Refutation of the Condition of Light for Vision
Refutation of the necessity of light for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that vision is not dependent on physical conditions like light, but rather on Allah's direct creation of the act of seeing.

Core Idea
Light is not necessary for vision; Allah creates sight directly.

Imam al-Nasafi presents a clear refutation of the Mu'tazilite view that light is a condition for vision. Using everyday examples, he shows that creatures see in darkness and fail to see in light, demonstrating that vision is not bound by physical causes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the structure: statement of refutation, then counterexamples, then theological conclusion.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite condition
The passage begins by stating 'their stipulation of light for the distance is false'. Identify what condition the Mu'tazilites held and how Nasafi rejects it.
The Mu'tazilites required light for vision; Nasafi argues this is false.
3
Understand the empirical examples
The author gives two examples: cats and insects seeing at night, and bats not seeing in daylight. Ask yourself: what do these examples prove?
These examples show that light is neither sufficient nor necessary for vision.
4
Grasp the theological conclusion
The final sentence establishes that vision occurs only when Allah creates 'vision in the eye'. Reflect on how this refutes natural causality.
This reinforces the Ash'ari/Maturidi view of direct divine causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا اشتراطهم الضوء في المسافة فاسد، إذ الظلمة لا تمنع من الرؤية عند القائلين بالرؤية للأشياء، روي عن أبي العباس القلانسي حكى عنه ابن فورك في كتابه اختلاف الشيخين، وذلك ليس مما يعتمد عليه لأن الهرة وكثيرا من الحشرات والهوام يرى بالليل وإن وجد الظلام، والخفاش لا يرى ببصره بالنهار، وإن وجد الضوء فلا اعتماد على الضوء وغيره، بل متى خلق الله تعالى الرؤية في البصر، حصل الوقوف على المرئي.
Likewise, their stipulation of light for the distance is false, because darkness does not prevent vision according to those who affirm vision of things. It is narrated from Abu al-'Abbas al-Qalanisi, Ibn Furak narrated from him in his book 'The Difference of the Two Shaykhs', but that (report) is not something to rely on because the cat and many insects and vermin see at night even though darkness exists, and the bat does not see with its eye during the day even though light exists. So there is no reliance on light or other [conditions]. Rather, whenever Allah Most High creates vision in the eye, perception of the seen object occurs.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that light is a necessary condition for vision. The author first mentions a report from al-Qalanisi that is not reliable, then provides empirical counterexamples: cats and many insects see at night in darkness, and bats do not see in daylight. This shows that light is not a necessary condition. Vision occurs directly by Allah's creation of the act of seeing in the eye, regardless of light or darkness. The author emphasizes that reliance should be placed solely on Allah's creative act, not on physical conditions.

Key Terms
الضوء
الظلمة
الرؤية
الخلق
البصر
الهرة
الخفاش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That vision is a natural process independent of God's direct creation of the act of seeing.
Correction: Vision occurs only when Allah directly creates the act of seeing, regardless of light or darkness.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Nasafi, what does the example of the cat seeing at night demonstrate?
A) That light is not a necessary condition for vision
B) That cats have special eyes
C) That darkness contains some light
D) That vision is by touch

Key Takeaway
Vision is directly created by Allah, not dependent on physical conditions like light.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on physical conditions like light to see, do you acknowledge that Allah is the true Creator of your sight? How would your gratitude change if you reminded yourself of this every time you open your eyes?
?
The passage shows that animals see in darkness and fail to see in light, proving conditions are irrelevant. Are there situations in your life where you assume something is impossible because conditions aren't right? Will you trust Allah to create the outcome regardless?
?
If vision is a direct act of Allah, then every glance you take is a miracle. How would your awareness of God's presence increase if you paused to thank Him for each moment of sight, especially the ones you take for granted?

The Decisive Refutation: Allah Sees Us
Imam al-Nasafi's concluding refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi uses the fact that Allah sees us as a decisive refutation of the Mu'tazila's arguments.

Core Idea
Allah seeing us is the ultimate refutation of those who deny His vision.

After dismantling various Mu'tazilite objections, Imam al-Nasafi delivers the final, conclusive argument: Allah Himself sees us. This simple yet profound fact overturns all their sophistries. Let's examine this decisive point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic sentence once, then read the literal English translation. Notice the structure: 'Then, what invalidates all... is that Allah sees us.'
This is the concluding sentence of a longer discussion.
2
Identify the claim and its opponent
Identify the claim: Allah sees us. Identify the opponent: the Mu'tazila, who deny that Allah can see or be seen.
The Mu'tazila argued that vision requires certain conditions (like facing, distance) which cannot apply to Allah.
3
Reflect on the refutation's power
Ask yourself: why does the fact that Allah sees us invalidate all previous sophistries? The answer lies in the certainty of revelation and rational necessity: if Allah sees us, then all arguments that say He cannot see are false.
This passage is a verbal signpost that the preceding arguments have been decisively answered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي يبطل جميع ما سبق ذكره من شبهات الخصوم أن الله تعالى يرانا خلافا لقول المعتزلة
Then, what invalidates all the previously mentioned sophistries of the opponents is that Allah Most High sees us, contrary to the statement of the Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This short passage serves as the concluding argument. After refuting various specific claims, the author presents a decisive refutation: the fact that Allah sees us is itself a refutation of all the arguments of the opponents. This is because the Mu'tazila deny that Allah can see or be seen, but the author asserts that Allah indeed sees us. This belief is based on revelation and rational proof, and it overturns all the false notions they proposed.

Key Terms
يبطل
شبهات
الخصوم
المعتزلة
يرانا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the Mu'tazilite view on vision is merely a philosophical position without major theological consequences.
Correction: This passage shows that the Mu'tazilite denial of Allah's vision is a serious theological error, and affirming that Allah sees us is a decisive refutation that invalidates all their arguments.

Checkpoint
What does Imam al-Nasafi say invalidates all the sophistries of the opponents?
A) That Allah sees us
B) That the ray is a body
C) That sound is an accident
D) That light is necessary for vision

Key Takeaway
Allah's seeing us is a foundational truth that decisively invalidates all arguments denying His vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that Allah sees you at every moment, what one habit would you be forced to change starting today?
?
The Mu'tazila used their intellect to deny that Allah sees you. What clever excuses do you make to ignore that Allah is seeing you right now?
?
Allah's seeing you is a decisive truth that invalidates all excuses. Will you let this truth govern your actions today, or will you treat it as just a theoretical belief?

God's Vision as the Ultimate Refutation
The author provides the positive proof that refutes all previous arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fact that God sees us without physical conditions refutes the opponents' arguments and why the conditions of vision in the physical world are attributes of existence, not of cause or condition.

Core Idea
God's vision of us proves that physical conditions are irrelevant for His sight.

The author now delivers the decisive argument that overturns all previous objections. He asserts that God sees us, and this vision occurs without any physical intermediaries. This simple truth exposes the flaw in the opponents' reasoning: they mistakenly assumed that the conditions of human vision apply to God. Let's see how this argument works and what it reveals about the status of those conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the English translation. Pay attention to the flow: the author first states the refutation, then explains the nature of the conditions.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the core assertion: God sees us without distance, connection of a ray, or impression in an organ. Understand why this negates all previous arguments.
This is the positive proof that the opponents cannot escape.
3
Understand the distinction
Note the final sentence: the conditions found in the witnessed world are attributes of existence (of created beings), not attributes of cause or condition. This clarifies that such conditions do not apply to God.
This distinction avoids the mistake of transferring physical conditions to the divine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي يبطل جميع ما سبق ذكره من شبهات الخصوم أن الله تعالى يرانا ولا مسافة بيننا وبينه ولا اتصال شعاع ولا انطباع المرئي في الآلة لتعاليه عن الرؤية بالآلة وهذا ما لا محيص لهم عنه. وتبين بتحقيق رؤية الله تعالى إيانا أن ما وجدوه في الشاهد من أوصاف الوجود لا من أوصاف العلة أو الشرط.
Then that which invalidates all the previous mentioned doubts from the arguments of the opponents is that God, exalted, sees us, and there is no distance between us and Him, nor connection of a ray, nor impression of the seen in the organ, due to His transcendence above vision by an organ, and this is inescapable for them. And it became clear by investigating God's vision of us that what they found in the witnessed world are attributes of existence, not attributes of cause or condition.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This passage provides the positive proof that refutes all previous arguments of the opponents. The author asserts that God sees us. He then explains that this vision does not require any physical conditions such as distance between seer and seen, a ray of light connecting them, or the impression of the object in the eye, because God is transcendent and does not see through an organ. This refutation is conclusive for the opponents. Furthermore, by investigating God's vision of us, it becomes clear that the conditions they find in the physical world (the 'witnessed') are attributes of existence (i.e., of created beings), not attributes of the cause or the condition. In other words, these conditions apply to contingent beings, not to God. Thus, the claim that God cannot see without these conditions is invalid.

Key Terms
الرؤية
المسافة
اتصال الشعاع
انطباع المرئي
الآلة
الشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that God's vision requires physical conditions like distance, light reflection, or an organ.
Correction: God sees us without any such conditions because He is transcendent and does not see through an organ; the conditions found in physical sight are attributes of created existence, not of the cause or condition itself.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim invalidates all previous arguments of the opponents?
A) That God sees us without distance or ray
B) That the conditions of vision are impossible for God
C) That the opponents' arguments are based on ignorance
D) That the Mu'tazila are mistaken in their theology

Key Takeaway
God's vision of us is unconditioned, and the physical requirements of sight are merely attributes of created existence, not necessary conditions for vision as such.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God sees you right now without any barrier. He sees your thoughts, your hidden actions, your heart. Does this truth stop you from sinning in private? Or do you live as if He does not see? What will you change today to live in constant awareness of His unconditioned sight?
?
You often impose conditions on God: 'If God does this, then I will believe' or 'If only God would show me a sign.' But the lesson teaches that God's actions are not bound by the conditions of creation. Where in your life have you been putting God in a box, demanding He act according to your expectations? Will you release that demand and submit to His unconditioned will?
?
The opponents in this lesson were refuted because they judged God by the standards of creation. When you pray, do you expect God to answer in ways that make sense to you, or are you truly open to His unconditioned response? What is one prayer you have been afraid to pray because you cannot control the answer?

Proof that Vision is Distinct from Knowledge
Nasafi proves ru'ya is distinct from 'ilm using sensory examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi proves that the attribute of vision (ru'ya) is distinct from the attribute of knowledge ('ilm) through sensory experience and logical reasoning.

Core Idea
Vision and knowledge are not the same; one can have knowledge without sight.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents a compelling argument to distinguish between two divine attributes: vision (ru'ya) and knowledge ('ilm). He uses everyday sensory experiences to show that knowledge can exist without vision, and vice versa, proving that they are separate realities. This distinction is crucial for understanding God's attributes correctly and refuting those who deny God's vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the argument flow. Pay attention to the examples used.
2
Understand the blind man example
Nasafi starts with a hypothetical: a blind man can know a color through touch, but remains blind. This shows knowledge without vision.
3
Analyze the closing eyes experiment
When you close your eyes while seeing an object, vision ceases but knowledge persists. This proves they are different.
4
Connect to divine attributes
Nasafi then applies this to God: He sees without direction, and denying His vision implies blindness, which is impossible.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument ultimately defends God's attribute of sight against those who claim transcendence but end up attributing defects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا لو كان الرسول عليه السلام أخبر أعمى بلون جسم عرفه الأعمى باللمس حصل له العلم بذلك واجتماع الجهل مع العلم بشيء واحد من جهة واحدة واجتماع رؤيته والعمى عنه محال ممتنع ، وبثبوت العلم بالمرئي زال الجهل وما زال العمى ولو كانا شيئا واحدا لما تصور، إذ زوال الشيء مع بقائه مستحيل. والذي يقرر هذا أن من رأى شيئا ثم غض عينيه والمرئي بعد بين يديه تبدلت حالتته لا محالة، وانعدمت منه في هذه الحالة صفة كانت موجودة قبل تغميض عينيه وما انعدم العلم بذلك الشيء فإنه عالم به وبصفاته وهيئته بعد التفحيص كما هو عالم به قبل التغميض ، فكان المنعدم هو الرؤية وانعدامها مع بقاء العلم دليل أنها معنى وراء العلم ثم لو فتح عينيه بعد ذلك حصلت له صفة كانت منعدمة في حالة التغميض وهي الرؤية ولم يحصل العلم لأنه كان حاصلا في حالة التغميض، فدل أن الرؤية معنى وراء العلم والله يرانا لا عن جهة. ولم يحصل من هذا المنع لهؤلاء الملحدين إلا تكذيب الله تعالى فيما وصف به نفسه، وتكذيب رسله عليهم السلام بما وصفوا به ربهم، ونسبة العمى إليه - تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا إذ الحي لا يخلو عن اتصافه بالبصر أو العمى، فإذا نفوا عنه البصر وصفوه بالعمى. ضرورة. وهو من أعظم أمارات الحدوث ونفى أمارات الحدوث والنقيصة أولى من نفي الرؤية عنه. كبيرا، ثم إن هؤلاء مع هذا ينسبون نفسهم إلى التنزيه والتوحيد مع إثباتهم أفحش العيوب وأوضح دلالات الحدوث.
And likewise, if the Messenger, peace be upon him, informed a blind man about the color of a body which the blind man knew by touch, he would obtain knowledge of that, and the combination of ignorance with knowledge of one thing from one aspect, and the combination of his vision and blindness with respect to it are impossible and inconceivable. And by the affirmation of knowledge of the visible thing, ignorance ceases, but blindness does not cease. And if they were one thing, it would not be conceivable, because the removal of a thing while it remains is impossible. And that which establishes this is that whoever sees something, then closes his eyes while the visible object is still before him, his state changes necessarily, and in this state a quality that existed before closing his eyes ceases, but knowledge of that thing does not cease; for he is knowledgeable of it, its attributes, and its condition after investigation, just as he was knowledgeable of it before closing his eyes. So that which ceased is vision, and its cessation with the persistence of knowledge is evidence that it is a meaning beyond knowledge. Then if he opens his eyes after that, a quality that was absent in the state of closing his eyes is obtained—which is vision—and knowledge is not obtained because it was already present in the state of closing his eyes. So this indicates that vision is a meaning beyond knowledge, and God sees us without direction. And from this refutation, these atheists obtain nothing but the denial of God regarding what He described Himself with, and the denial of His messengers, peace be upon them, regarding what they described their Lord with, and the attribution of blindness to Him—exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say, greatly—since the living being cannot be free from being described with sight or blindness. So if they negate sight from Him, they describe Him with blindness necessarily. And it is one of the greatest signs of contingency, and negating the signs of contingency and deficiency is more appropriate than negating vision from Him. Greatly, then these people, along with this, attribute themselves to transcendence and monotheism while they affirm the most vile defects and the clearest indications of contingency.
Source pages p. 642

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the attribute of vision (ru'ya) is distinct from the attribute of knowledge ('ilm). Nasafi begins with a hypothetical: if a blind person learns about a color through touch, he gains knowledge but remains blind. This shows that blindness and knowledge can coexist. Then he moves to an experiential argument: when a person sees an object and then closes his eyes, his state changes—the vision ceases, but his knowledge of the object remains. If vision and knowledge were identical, the removal of vision would require the removal of knowledge, which is not the case. Conversely, when he opens his eyes, he regains vision but does not gain new knowledge because it was already present. Thus, vision is an additional meaning (ma'na) beyond knowledge. From this, Nasafi concludes that God sees us without direction (jiha). He then criticizes those who deny that God sees (the atheists), arguing that their denial leads to attributing blindness to God, which is a sign of contingency—something far worse than affirming vision. He ends by noting the hypocrisy of those who claim transcendence while affirming defects.

Key Terms
رؤية
علم
عمى
جهة
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think vision and knowledge are the same attribute.
Correction: The proof shows they are distinct because one can have knowledge without vision (e.g., blind person knowing color) and vision without new knowledge (opening eyes after closing).

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what does the persistence of knowledge when closing one's eyes demonstrate?
A) That vision and knowledge are identical.
B) That vision is a meaning distinct from knowledge.
C) That knowledge depends on vision.
D) That blindness is a form of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Vision (ru'ya) is a divine attribute distinct from knowledge ('ilm), as proven by the persistence of knowledge when vision is temporarily lost.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself rationalizing away God's attributes because they don't fit your limited understanding? In what specific area of your life are you most tempted to 'transcend' God by denying what He has affirmed about Himself?
?
Nasafi says that denying vision to God necessarily implies blindness—a deficiency. When you describe God, do you ever unconsciously strip Him of His attributes under the guise of 'tanzih'? Would you rather have a God who sees you or one who is blind to your struggles?
?
The blind man gained knowledge but remained blind. In your spiritual journey, are you content with just knowledge about God, or do you long for the 'vision'—the direct awareness of His presence? What would change if you truly believed He sees you right now?

Refuting the Basriyyun Instrument Argument
Nasafi refutes the Basriyyun distinction by analyzing the determinant for direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Basriyyun argument that vision with an instrument requires direction is invalid, and how Nasafi refutes it using the example of the heart.

Core Idea
The instrument does not necessitate direction, because the heart knows God without direction.

The Basriyyun (Mu'tazila of Basra) accept that God sees us without an instrument, but they claim that our seeing God requires an instrument (the eye) and therefore necessitates direction. In this passage, Nasafi systematically dismantles this distinction by asking: what is the determinant (muqtada) for direction? Is it vision itself or the instrument? He shows both possibilities fail, using clear counterexamples.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Familiarize yourself with the Basriyyun's distinction between God seeing us (without instrument) and us seeing God (with instrument). Notice their analogy of the hand.
2
Identify the two possible determinants
Nasafi considers two possibilities: the determinant is vision itself, or the determinant is the instrument. Understand why these are the only logical possibilities.
This is a classic qiyas tafsīq (bifurcation) argument.
3
Apply the refutation to each case
If the determinant is vision, then God's seeing us would require direction, which is false. If the determinant is the instrument, then our knowledge of God (obtained through the heart) would require direction, which is also false.
Notice how Nasafi uses knowledge (ʿilm) as a parallel example.
4
Conclude the argument is corrupt
Since both possibilities lead to falsehood, the Basriyyun objection is invalid. Nasafi also preempts a potential rejoinder that the combination of vision and instrument could necessitate direction, and refutes it by showing that non-necessitating things do not become necessitating by combination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبصرية منهم يسلمون أن الله تعالى يرانا ولكن يفرقون بين رؤيته إيانا ورؤيتنا إياه، فيقولون : إنه تعالى يرانا لا بالآلة فيتحقق ويتصور بدون الجهة والمقابلة، فأما نحن فنراه بالآلة فلا يتصور إلا في الجهة، لأن الآلة استعمالها إلا في حيز معلوم كمن فعل بنا بيده لا يمكنه أن يفعل إلا في جهة مخصوصة فكذا هذا. والجواب عنه : أن المقتضى للجهة إن كان هو الرؤية فباطل برؤية الله تعالى إيانا، وإن كان هو الآلة فباطل بعلمنا الله تعالى فإنه يحصل بالآلة وهو القلب وهو جسم، ولا يقتضي الجهة والمقابلة ولا ثالث هاهنا، وكل واحد من الأمرين غير مقتضى للجهة، دل أن هذا الاعتراض فاسد ولا يقال كل واحد منهما لا يقتضي الجهة، وإذا اجتمعا اقتضيا لأن ما ليس بمقتضي للشيء إذا اجتمع مع بمقتض له لا يقتضيان لأنهما بذواتهما لا يقتضيان ولا أثر للاجتماع، وبهذا يبطل قولهم : إن المرئي إما أن يكون مقابلا كالجسم أو حالا في المقابلة كالألوان والأكوان أو ما له حكم المقابلة كالمرئى في المرآة من صور الأشياء. فإن الله تعالى يرى المرئيات لا بهذه الوجوه على أن هذا الكلام باطل فإن المقابلة لو كانت شرطا لكان العرض لا يرى ولو كان الحلول في المقابلة شرطا لكان لا ترى الجواهر ولا ما له
And the Basriyyun among them acknowledge that God sees us, but they differentiate between His seeing us and our seeing Him. They say: He, exalted, sees us without an instrument, so it is actualized and conceivable without direction and confrontation. As for us, we see Him with an instrument, so it is not conceivable except in a direction, because the instrument cannot be used except in a known space, like one who acts with his hand can only act in a specific direction. So likewise this. The answer to that: If the determinant for direction is vision, then it is invalid by God's seeing us; and if it is the instrument, then it is invalid by our knowledge of God, for it is obtained through an instrument—the heart—which is a body, and it does not necessitate direction or confrontation. There is no third possibility here. Each of the two matters does not necessitate direction, so this shows that this objection is corrupt. And it cannot be said that each of them does not necessitate direction, but when they combine they necessitate it, because that which does not necessitate a thing, when combined with something that necessitates it, does not necessitate it, since they by themselves do not necessitate and there is no effect of combination. By this, their saying is invalid: that the visible object is either confronting like a body, or in a state of confrontation like colors and accidents, or that which has the ruling of confrontation like the images of things in a mirror. Indeed, God sees visible things not in these ways. Moreover, this statement is invalid, because if confrontation were a condition, then accidents would not be seen; and if being in a state of confrontation were a condition, then substances would not be seen, nor that which has...
Source pages p. 643

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by the Basriyyun (Mu'tazila of Basra) and its refutation. The Basriyyun accept that God sees us, but they claim a difference: God sees us without an instrument (ala), so no direction (jiha) or confrontation (muqabala) is needed; but we see with an instrument (the eye), so we can only see God if He is in a direction. They use an analogy of a hand that can only act in a specific direction. Nasafi refutes by analyzing the determinant (muqtada) for direction. If the determinant is vision itself, then it is refuted by the fact that God sees us without direction. If it is the instrument, then it is refuted by our knowledge of God, which is obtained through an instrument (the heart, a body) yet does not require direction. Both possibilities fail, so the objection is invalid. He also rejects the idea that the two together could necessitate direction, because non-necessitating things do not become necessitating by combination. Finally, he attacks their classification of visible objects: either confronting (like bodies), or in a state of confrontation (like colors and accidents), or having the ruling of confrontation (like mirror images). He argues that if confrontation were a condition, then accidents (which are not bodies) would not be seen; and if being in a state of confrontation were a condition, then substances (which are not in a state of confrontation) would not be seen. Thus, the objection fails completely.

Key Terms
آلة
جهة
مقابلة
رؤية
علم
قلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi use to refute the claim that the instrument necessitates direction?
A) The fact that God sees without an instrument.
B) The fact that knowledge of God is obtained through the heart without direction.
C) The analogy of the hand.
D) The classification of visible objects.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the Basriyyun argument by showing that neither vision nor the instrument necessitates direction, using the counterexample of God's vision and human knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Your heart is the instrument through which you know God. Are you more reliant on your external senses than on your inner knowledge? What one change in your daily routine can help you cultivate a direct, directionless awareness of His presence?
?
If God's vision of you is without direction, can there be any 'distance' between you and Him? How would your life change if you truly believed He is nearer to you than your jugular vein? What practice will you adopt to remind yourself of this reality every day?

Refuting the Classification of Visible Objects
Nasafi refutes the Basriyyun classification of visible objects by showing it excludes accidents or substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Basriyyun classification of visible objects (confronting, in a state of confrontation, or having its ruling) is invalid, and how Nasafi uses the examples of accidents and substances to refute it.

Core Idea
If confrontation were a condition for visibility, accidents would not be seen.

Having refuted the instrument-direction argument, Nasafi now attacks the Basriyyun's classification of visible objects. The Basriyyun claimed that any visible object must either be confronting (like a body), in a state of confrontation (like an accident in a body), or have the ruling of confrontation (like an image in a mirror). Nasafi shows that this classification is self-defeating because it would exclude many things that are actually seen.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Basriyyun classification
Read the passage to grasp the three types of visible objects according to the Basriyyun: confronting like a body, in state of confrontation like colors/accidents, or having the ruling of confrontation like mirror images.
2
Analyze Nasafi's refutation
Nasafi points out that if confrontation were a condition, then accidents (which are not bodies) would not be seen. If being in a state of confrontation were a condition, then substances (which are not accidents) would not be seen. Therefore, the classification is invalid.
This shows that the conditions are too restrictive.
3
Conclude the overall refutation
By showing the classification is self-defeating, Nasafi completes his refutation of the Basriyyun objection. God sees all things without any of these conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبصرية منهم يسلمون أن الله تعالى يرانا ولكن يفرقون بين رؤيته إيانا ورؤيتنا إياه، فيقولون : إنه تعالى يرانا لا بالآلة فيتحقق ويتصور بدون الجهة والمقابلة، فأما نحن فنراه بالآلة فلا يتصور إلا في الجهة، لأن الآلة استعمالها إلا في حيز معلوم كمن فعل بنا بيده لا يمكنه أن يفعل إلا في جهة مخصوصة فكذا هذا. والجواب عنه : أن المقتضى للجهة إن كان هو الرؤية فباطل برؤية الله تعالى إيانا، وإن كان هو الآلة فباطل بعلمنا الله تعالى فإنه يحصل بالآلة وهو القلب وهو جسم، ولا يقتضي الجهة والمقابلة ولا ثالث هاهنا، وكل واحد من الأمرين غير مقتضى للجهة، دل أن هذا الاعتراض فاسد ولا يقال كل واحد منهما لا يقتضي الجهة، وإذا اجتمعا اقتضيا لأن ما ليس بمقتضي للشيء إذا اجتمع مع بمقتض له لا يقتضيان لأنهما بذواتهما لا يقتضيان ولا أثر للاجتماع، وبهذا يبطل قولهم : إن المرئي إما أن يكون مقابلا كالجسم أو حالا في المقابلة كالألوان والأكوان أو ما له حكم المقابلة كالمرئى في المرآة من صور الأشياء. فإن الله تعالى يرى المرئيات لا بهذه الوجوه على أن هذا الكلام باطل فإن المقابلة لو كانت شرطا لكان العرض لا يرى ولو كان الحلول في المقابلة شرطا لكان لا ترى الجواهر ولا ما له
And the Basriyyun among them acknowledge that God sees us, but they differentiate between His seeing us and our seeing Him. They say: He, exalted, sees us without an instrument, so it is actualized and conceivable without direction and confrontation. As for us, we see Him with an instrument, so it is not conceivable except in a direction, because the instrument cannot be used except in a known space, like one who acts with his hand can only act in a specific direction. So likewise this. The answer to that: If the determinant for direction is vision, then it is invalid by God's seeing us; and if it is the instrument, then it is invalid by our knowledge of God, for it is obtained through an instrument—the heart—which is a body, and it does not necessitate direction or confrontation. There is no third possibility here. Each of the two matters does not necessitate direction, so this shows that this objection is corrupt. And it cannot be said that each of them does not necessitate direction, but when they combine they necessitate it, because that which does not necessitate a thing, when combined with something that necessitates it, does not necessitate it, since they by themselves do not necessitate and there is no effect of combination. By this, their saying is invalid: that the visible object is either confronting like a body, or in a state of confrontation like colors and accidents, or that which has the ruling of confrontation like the images of things in a mirror. Indeed, God sees visible things not in these ways. Moreover, this statement is invalid, because if confrontation were a condition, then accidents would not be seen; and if being in a state of confrontation were a condition, then substances would not be seen, nor that which has...
Source pages p. 643

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by the Basriyyun (Mu'tazila of Basra) and its refutation. The Basriyyun accept that God sees us, but they claim a difference: God sees us without an instrument (ala), so no direction (jiha) or confrontation (muqabala) is needed; but we see with an instrument (the eye), so we can only see God if He is in a direction. They use an analogy of a hand that can only act in a specific direction. Nasafi refutes by analyzing the determinant (muqtada) for direction. If the determinant is vision itself, then it is refuted by the fact that God sees us without direction. If it is the instrument, then it is refuted by our knowledge of God, which is obtained through an instrument (the heart, a body) yet does not require direction. Both possibilities fail, so the objection is invalid. He also rejects the idea that the two together could necessitate direction, because non-necessitating things do not become necessitating by combination. Finally, he attacks their classification of visible objects: either confronting (like bodies), or in a state of confrontation (like colors and accidents), or having the ruling of confrontation (like mirror images). He argues that if confrontation were a condition, then accidents (which are not bodies) would not be seen; and if being in a state of confrontation were a condition, then substances (which are not in a state of confrontation) would not be seen. Thus, the objection fails completely.

Refutation of the Claim that Vision Requires Direction
Objection and refutation concerning direction for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why direction is not a necessary condition for vision of God, based on the refutation of the argument that instrument-based vision requires direction.

Core Idea
Direction is not required for vision; the instrument does not necessitate it.

This passage addresses an objection raised by those who argue that since God sees without an instrument, His vision is directionless, but human vision requires an instrument and thus direction. The author refutes this by showing that neither vision itself nor the instrument alone necessitates direction, and their combination does not either. Let's examine the reasoning carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage starting with 'ورؤيتنا إياه' until the objection is presented. Notice how the objectors argue that our vision of God requires direction because it uses an instrument.
The objection is based on analogy with physical actions like using a hand.
2
Identify the two possible causes
The refutation tests two possible causes for direction: vision itself or the instrument. Understand that these are the only two options considered.
3
Refutation of each cause
See how each cause is refuted: if vision, God's vision of us would require direction (false); if instrument, our knowledge of God via the heart would require direction (false). Both are invalid.
Knowledge through the heart is given as an example of an instrument that does not require direction.
4
Why combination doesn't help
The objector might claim that the combination of vision and instrument necessitates direction. The author argues that since each alone does not necessitate, their combination cannot produce a new effect.
The inherent nature of each remains unchanged, so combination has no impact.
5
Grasp the conclusion
Understand that the objection is declared corrupt because no valid necessitator for direction is found. The vision of God remains possible without direction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورؤيتنا إياه، فيقولون : إنه تعالى يرانا لا بالآلة فيتحقق ويتصور بدون الجهة والمقابلة، فأما نحن فنراه بالآلة فلا يتصور إلا في الجهة، لأن الآلة استعمالها إلا في حيز معلوم كمن فعل بنا بيده لا يمكنه أن يفعل إلا في جهة مخصوصة فكذا هذا. [الرد عليهم] والجواب عنه : أن المقتضى للجهة إن كان هو الرؤية فباطل برؤية الله تعالى إيانا، وإن كان هو الآلة فباطل بعلمنا الله تعالى فإنه يحصل بالآلة وهو القلب وهو جسم، ولا يقتضي الجهة والمقابلة ولا ثالث هاهنا، وكل واحد من الأمرين غير مقتضى للجهة، دل أن هذا الاعتراض فاسد ولا يقال كل واحد منهما لا يقتضي الجهة، وإذا اجتمعا اقتضيا لأن ما ليس بمقتضي للشيء إذا اجتمع مع بمقتض له لا يقتضيان لأنهما بذواتهما لا يقتضيان ولا أثر للاجتماع.
And our vision of Him, they say: Indeed He, exalted, sees us not by an instrument, so it is realized and conceived without direction and opposition. But as for us, we see Him by an instrument, so it is not conceived except in direction, because the instrument is not used except in a known space, like one who acts upon us with his hand, he cannot act except in a specific direction. Thus this. [The refutation of them] And the reply to it: that the cause of direction, if it is vision, then it is invalidated by God's vision of us; and if it is the instrument, then it is invalidated by our knowledge of God, for it occurs by an instrument, which is the heart, and it is a body, and it does not necessitate direction and opposition; and there is no third here; and each of the two things does not necessitate direction. So this indicates that this objection is corrupt; and it cannot be said that each of them does not necessitate direction, but when they combine they necessitate it, because what does not necessitate a thing, when combined with what necessitates, does not necessitate, because they inherently do not necessitate and combination has no effect.
Source pages p. 643

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its response. The objectors argue that since God sees us without an instrument, His vision is without direction, but our vision of God would require an instrument and thus direction. The author refutes by analyzing the cause of direction: if it is vision itself, then God's vision of us would require direction, which is false; if it is the instrument, then our knowledge of God (which occurs via the heart, a body) would require direction, but it does not. Therefore, neither factor alone necessitates direction, and their combination also does not necessitate because combination does not produce a new effect. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
الجهة (direction)
المقابلة (opposition/facing)
الآلة (instrument)
الرؤية (vision)
المقتضى (cause/necessitator)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All vision requires direction because it involves an instrument.
Correction: Vision without direction is possible for God, and even instrument-based acts like knowledge do not always require direction.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for rejecting the claim that our vision of God requires direction because we see with an instrument?
A) The instrument itself does not necessitate direction, as seen in knowledge via the heart.
B) God's vision of us proves direction is necessary for all vision.
C) The heart is not a body, so it does not require direction.
D) The objection is rejected because it comes from heretics.

Key Takeaway
Direction is not a necessary condition for vision because neither the act of seeing nor the instrument used necessitates it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The objection assumes direction is necessary for vision because of limitation. Do you still have hidden assumptions about God based on your own limitations? Confess one such assumption to yourself and repent.

Opponents' Classification of Visibles Refuted
Presents opponents' classification and asserts its invalidity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' classification of visible objects and why the author declares it invalid.

Core Idea
Vision does not require spatial opposition or being in a state of opposition.

The author begins by presenting the classification of visible objects proposed by his opponents. They divide visibles into three categories: bodies, accidents, and images in mirrors. He then asserts that God sees all things without these conditions and declares the classification false. This lesson examines that initial claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It states the opponents' classification and the author's declaration of falsehood.
Notice the three categories: opposite, in state of opposition, and having ruling of opposition.
2
Identify the three categories
List the three types of visible objects according to the opponents. Compare them to the translation.
3
Reflect on the author's claim
Why does the author say 'this speech is false'? What reasons might he have? This sets up the detailed refutation in the next lesson.
The author asserts that God sees in ways not limited to these conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يبطل قولهم : إن المرئي إما أن يكون مقابلا كالجسم أو حالا في المقابلة كالألوان والأكوان أو ما له حكم المقابلة كالمرئى في المرآة من صور الأشياء. فإن الله تعالى يرى المرئيات لا بهذه الوجوه على أن هذا الكلام باطل فإن المقابلة لو كانت شرطا لكان العرض لا يرى ولو كان الحلول في المقابلة شرطا لكان لا ترى الجواهر ولا ما له حكم المقابلة حكم المقابلة، ولو كان كونه في حال المقابلة شرطا لكان المقابل والحال في المقابل لا يريان، ورؤية كل شيء من هذه الأشياء مع انعدام ما اقترن بصاحبه دل أن شيئا من ذلك ليس بشرط، ولو شرط اجتماعها لكان لا يرى شيء البتة، لاستحالة اجتماعها، ولو جعل أحد هذه الأشياء شرطا لا محالة فلابد لهم من إقامة الدليل ولا دليل لهم سوى الوجود وهذا باطل على ما بينا.
And by this, their statement is invalidated: that the visible is either opposite like a body, or in a state of opposition like colors and accidents, or what has the ruling of opposition like the images of things seen in a mirror. For God sees visible things not in these ways. Moreover, this speech is false, for if opposition were a condition, then the accident would not be seen; and if being in a state of opposition were a condition, then substances and what has the ruling of opposition would not be seen; and if its being in a state of opposition were a condition, then the opposite and that which is in a state of opposition would not be seen; and the vision of every one of these things with the absence of what is associated with its counterpart indicates that none of that is a condition. And if their combination were made a condition, then nothing would be seen at all, due to the impossibility of their combination; and if one of these things were made a condition necessarily, then they must provide a proof, and they have no proof other than existence, and this is false as we have shown.
Source pages p. 643

Commentary
This passage refutes the opponents' classification of visible objects into three categories: those opposite (like bodies), those in a state of opposition (like accidents), and those having the ruling of opposition (like images in mirrors). The author argues that if any of these were a condition for vision, then many things we actually see would not be seen. For example, if opposition were a condition, accidents (like colors) would not be seen because they are not opposite in themselves. If being in a state of opposition were a condition, then substances (jawahir) would not be seen. If being in a state of opposition were a condition for the opposite, then both the opposite and the thing in opposition would not be seen. Since we do see these things, none of these are conditions. Moreover, if all conditions were required, nothing could be seen because they cannot coexist. The opponents have no proof other than mere existence, which is invalid.

Key Terms
المقابلة (opposition)
الجسم (body)
العرض (accident)
الجوهر (substance)
الحلول في المقابلة (being in a state of opposition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Visible objects must be spatially opposite or in a state of opposition to be seen.
Correction: The author argues that God sees all things without these conditions, and subsequent reasoning shows these conditions are not necessary.

Checkpoint

B) In a state of opposition
C) Having ruling of opposition
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The opponents classify visibles into three categories, but the author declares this false because God sees without such conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Detailed Refutation of Conditions for Vision
Refutes each condition individually and the combination, and notes opponents have no proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses conditional reasoning to refute each proposed condition for vision and the impossibility of combining them.

Core Idea
If any condition were necessary, many things we see would be invisible.

After declaring the classification false, the author provides detailed reasoning. He tests each condition individually using a conditional argument: if X were a condition, then Y would not be seen. Since we do see Y, X cannot be a condition. He also considers the possibility of combining conditions and shows that would make nothing visible. This lesson unpacks these logical refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It contains several conditional statements and a final challenge.
Notice the pattern: 'if ... were a condition, then ... would not be seen'.
2
Identify each conditional argument
List each condition tested: opposition, being in a state of opposition, and the third nuanced condition. What is the consequence for each?
3
Understand the pattern
The author shows that the predicted non-vision contradicts our actual experience. Therefore, the condition is invalid.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Consider the combination and lack of proof
The author also argues that combining conditions leads to impossibility, and opponents have no valid proof. Reflect on why these are powerful refutations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يبطل قولهم : إن المرئي إما أن يكون مقابلا كالجسم أو حالا في المقابلة كالألوان والأكوان أو ما له حكم المقابلة كالمرئى في المرآة من صور الأشياء. فإن الله تعالى يرى المرئيات لا بهذه الوجوه على أن هذا الكلام باطل فإن المقابلة لو كانت شرطا لكان العرض لا يرى ولو كان الحلول في المقابلة شرطا لكان لا ترى الجواهر ولا ما له حكم المقابلة حكم المقابلة، ولو كان كونه في حال المقابلة شرطا لكان المقابل والحال في المقابل لا يريان، ورؤية كل شيء من هذه الأشياء مع انعدام ما اقترن بصاحبه دل أن شيئا من ذلك ليس بشرط، ولو شرط اجتماعها لكان لا يرى شيء البتة، لاستحالة اجتماعها، ولو جعل أحد هذه الأشياء شرطا لا محالة فلابد لهم من إقامة الدليل ولا دليل لهم سوى الوجود وهذا باطل على ما بينا.
And by this, their statement is invalidated: that the visible is either opposite like a body, or in a state of opposition like colors and accidents, or what has the ruling of opposition like the images of things seen in a mirror. For God sees visible things not in these ways. Moreover, this speech is false, for if opposition were a condition, then the accident would not be seen; and if being in a state of opposition were a condition, then substances and what has the ruling of opposition would not be seen; and if its being in a state of opposition were a condition, then the opposite and that which is in a state of opposition would not be seen; and the vision of every one of these things with the absence of what is associated with its counterpart indicates that none of that is a condition. And if their combination were made a condition, then nothing would be seen at all, due to the impossibility of their combination; and if one of these things were made a condition necessarily, then they must provide a proof, and they have no proof other than existence, and this is false as we have shown.

Commentary
This passage refutes the opponents' classification of visible objects into three categories: those opposite (like bodies), those in a state of opposition (like accidents), and those having the ruling of opposition (like images in mirrors). The author argues that if any of these were a condition for vision, then many things we actually see would not be seen. For example, if opposition were a condition, accidents (like colors) would not be seen because they are not opposite in themselves. If being in a state of opposition were a condition, then substances (jawahir) would not be seen. Moreover, if all conditions were required, nothing could be seen because they cannot coexist. The opponents have no proof other than mere existence, which is invalid.

المقابلة (opposition)
العرض (accident)
الجوهر (substance)
حكم المقابلة (ruling of opposition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponents might think that spatial conditions are necessary for any vision, including divine vision.
Correction: The author shows that these conditions cannot be necessary because that would lead to contradictions with observed vision.

Checkpoint

?

Refutation of Linguistic and Analogical Objections to Vision
The author refutes linguistic and analogical objections and concludes that direction is not intrinsic to vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes linguistic and analogical objections to the vision of God, and why direction and physical conditions are not intrinsic to vision.

Core Idea
Vision of God is not dependent on physical conditions like direction or opposition.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi addresses two final objections raised by those who deny the possibility of seeing God. The first objection is linguistic: the term 'vision' in Arabic implies certain physical conditions that cannot apply to God. The second is analogical: knowledge of the unseen must resemble knowledge of the witnessed. The author refutes both by showing that vision is defined by the act itself, not by accidental concomitants. Let's examine his arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the two objections being refuted.
The passage uses technical terms like 'القرائن الاتفاقية'.
2
Examine the first (linguistic) objection
The opponents claim that vision requires conditions like opposition, alignment, etc. Find how the author responds: he says these are only coincidental concomitants.
The author argues that vision is vision without them.
3
Examine the second (analogical) objection
The opponents draw an analogy with knowledge: the unseen must follow the witnessed. Find the author's counter: the same applies to vision, but the conditions are not necessary.
He points out that we see bodies in directions because they are in directions, not because direction is intrinsic to vision.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that God, being not finite and without direction, sees without direction. This establishes that vision of God is possible and different from physical vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يبطل قولهم : إن ما تدعونه أنه رؤية ليس برؤية إذ هي لا تعرف عند أهل اللغة فإن الله تعالى يرى ورؤيته ليست بمقابلة ولا محاذاة ولا اتصال شعاع ولا تقليب مقلة. وتبين أن هذه الأشياء ليست إلا من القرائن الاتفاقية والأوصاف الوجودية ، والرؤية رؤية بدونها. وكذا قولهم : إن العلم بالغائب لا يخرج عن الوجه الذي به يعلم الشاهد. قلنا : وكذا الرؤية فإنها كما كانت في حق الشاهد ضرورة كانت في حق الغائب كذا. وما ذكر من المسافة والمقابلة واتصال الشعاع قد أبطلنا أن يكون شيء منه من لوازم الرؤية بل كل شيء يرى كما هو وعلى ما هو، فلما كانت الأجسام في الجهات منا وبيننا وبينها قواء رأيناها كما هي وعلى ما هي عليه، لا لأن الجهة والمقابلة من لوازم الرؤية. والله تعالى ليس بمتناه ولا منا بجهة فيرى كما هو يرانا هو لا عن جهة ولا في جهة.
And by this, their statement is invalidated: that what you claim is vision is not vision, since it is not known among linguists. For God sees, and His vision is not by opposition, nor alignment, nor connection of rays, nor turning of the pupil. And it is shown that these things are only coincidental concomitants and existential attributes, and vision is vision without them. And likewise their statement: that knowledge of the unseen does not depart from the manner by which the witnessed is known. We say: likewise vision, for just as it was in the case of the witnessed necessarily, it is in the case of the unseen likewise. And what was mentioned of distance, opposition, and connection of rays, we have already nullified that any of it is among the necessary concomitants of vision; rather, everything is seen as it is and according to what it is. Since bodies are in directions relative to us and between us and them there is void, we see them as they are and as they are, not because direction and opposition are among the necessary concomitants of vision. And God is not finite nor in a direction relative to us, so He sees as He is, as He sees us, not by direction nor in direction.
Source pages p. 644

Commentary
This passage addresses two final objections. First, the opponents claim that the vision we affirm for God is not called 'vision' in language because it lacks conditions like opposition, alignment, etc. The author responds that vision is independent of these accidental features; they are merely coincidental. Second, the opponents draw an analogy with knowledge, arguing that knowledge of the unseen must follow the same pattern as knowledge of the witnessed. The author retorts that the same applies to vision: just as vision in the witnessed world occurs necessarily (under certain conditions), in the unseen it occurs without those conditions. The author concludes by reiterating that distance, opposition, and rays are not essential to vision. We see bodies in directions because they are in directions relative to us, but that does not mean direction is intrinsic to vision. God, being unlimited and without direction, sees without direction, just as He sees us without direction.

Key Terms
الرؤية (vision)
اللغة (language)
القرائن الاتفاقية (coincidental concomitants)
العلم بالغائب (knowledge of the unseen)
المشاهد (the witnessed)
المسافة (distance)
اتصال الشعاع (connection of rays)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that vision of God in the afterlife will be exactly like physical vision in this world, requiring conditions like direction and distance.
Correction: The passage clarifies that physical conditions such as opposition, alignment, and distance are only coincidental concomitants of vision in the created world. They are not essential to the act of vision itself. God's vision is without direction or any physical condition.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the claim that vision of God is not called 'vision' linguistically?
A) He argues that linguistic definitions are irrelevant to theological reality.
B) He says that vision is defined by the act itself, not by accidental conditions like opposition or direction.
C) He cites a dictionary definition that includes God's vision.
D) He agrees and uses a different term.

Key Takeaway
The vision of God is not dependent on physical conditions like direction, opposition, or rays; these are merely coincidental concomitants in the created world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you accept that God's vision of you is not limited by space or direction, and yet you often act as if He does not see you in your private moments? How can you cultivate a constant awareness of His vision without needing physical signs as evidence?
?
You just read that physical conditions like opposition and distance are not essential to vision. Are there aspects of your faith that you have made dependent on physical, worldly conditions (like prayer being 'effective' only in a quiet room)? What would it mean for you to worship God without relying on such conditions?

5 min
Vision of God Does Not Require Physical Conditions
Nasafi refutes that facing, distance, or direction are necessary for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that conditions like facing, alignment, and ray connection are not necessary for seeing God, and how this defends the possibility of beatific vision.

Core Idea
God's vision is unlike physical vision; it needs no direction or distance.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles a common objection: that vision requires certain physical conditions such as facing, alignment, or a ray. He shows these are merely incidental to physical sight and cannot be applied to God. This clears the way for affirming that God can be seen without these limitations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Nasafi systematically lists and refutes each condition.
The passage uses a series of conditional arguments (if...then...).
2
Identify the conditions refuted
List the conditions that Nasafi mentions: confrontation, alignment, connection of a ray, rotation of eyeball. See how he argues against each.
He points out contradictions and lack of evidence.
3
Understand the refutation logic
Nasafi uses three arguments: (1) if confrontation were a condition, confronting parties wouldn't see each other; (2) seeing occurs even when some conditions are absent; (3) if all were required, nothing would be seen since they can't combine. Then he notes opponents have no proof.
The argument is based on absurd consequences.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Nasafi concludes that these conditions are merely coincidental (قرائن اتفاقية) and not essential. Thus vision of God does not require them.
This defends the theological doctrine that God can be seen in the afterlife without physical limitations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم المقابلة، ولو كان كونه في حال المقابلة شرطا لكان المقابل والحال في المقابل لا يريان، ورؤية كل شيء من هذه الأشياء مع انعدام ما اقترن بصاحبه دل أن شيئا من ذلك ليس بشرط، ولو شرط اجتماعها لكان لا يرى شيء البتة، لاستحالة اجتماعها، ولو جعل أحد هذه الأشياء شرطا لا محالة فلابد لهم من إقامة الدليل ولا دليل لهم سوى الوجود وهذا باطل على ما بينا. وبهذا يبطل قولهم : إن ما تدعونه أنه رؤية ليس برؤية إذ هي لا تعرف عند أهل اللغة فإن الله تعالى يرى ورؤيته ليست بمقابلة ولا محاذاة ولا اتصال شعاع ولا تقليب مقلة. وتبين أن هذه الأشياء ليست إلا من القرائن الاتفاقية والأوصاف الوجودية () ، والرؤية رؤية بدونها.
The ruling on confrontation: if being in a state of confrontation were a condition, then the one confronting and the one in a state of confrontation would not see each other. And the seeing of every one of these things when what is associated with its companion is absent indicates that none of that is a condition. And if their combination were a condition, nothing at all would be seen, because their combination is impossible. And if one of these things is necessarily made a condition, they must produce evidence, and they have no evidence except existence, and that is false as we have shown. And by this their statement is refuted: 'What you claim to be vision is not vision, for it is not known among the people of language.' Indeed, God the Exalted is seen, and His seeing is not by confrontation, nor alignment, nor connection of a ray, nor rotation of an eyeball. And it is shown that these things are only coincidental concomitants and existential attributes, and vision is vision without them.
Source pages p. 644

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim that vision necessarily requires certain physical conditions such as confrontation (المقابلة), alignment (المحاذاة), connection of a ray (اتصال الشعاع), and rotation of the eyeball (تقليب المقلة). The argument proceeds by noting that if confrontation were a condition, then the confronting and the confronted would not see each other (implying a contradiction). Then it observes that seeing occurs even when these conditions are absent for some objects, showing they are not necessary. If all were required, nothing would be seen because they cannot combine. The opponents have no proof except sheer existence, which is invalid. Thus Nasafi concludes that these conditions are merely coincidental, not essential to vision. God is seen without them, and the term 'vision' in language does not require these physical contingencies.

Key Terms
الرؤية
المقابلة
المحاذاة
اتصال الشعاع
تقليب المقلة
القرائن الاتفاقية
الأوصاف الوجودية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision necessarily requires physical direction and distance.
Correction: Physical conditions like facing and alignment are only incidental to physical sight; God's vision does not require them.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what indicates that confrontation is not a condition for vision?
A) The fact that confronting and confronted cannot see each other if it were a condition.
B) The fact that objects are seen even when the associated condition is absent.
C) The impossibility of combining all conditions.
D) The lack of evidence from the opponents.

Key Takeaway
God can be seen without physical conditions; the conditions of physical sight are merely incidental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you realize that your habit of imagining God in physical terms—like a direction or distance—is a subtle form of shirk? How has this lesson exposed that tendency in your own heart? What concrete step will you take today to purify your concept of God from such limitations?
?
When you think of 'seeing God' in the afterlife, do you subconsciously picture it as a physical sight? This passage demands you abandon that. Are you willing to accept that God's reality entirely surpasses your sensory expectations? What comfort or discomfort does that bring you?
?
The opponents had no evidence for their conditions except 'existence'—meaning they assumed physical laws are absolute. Where in your own life do you impose worldly limitations on God without proof? Name one such assumption and commit to replacing it with what this lesson teaches: that God is seen without conditions.

Analogy Between Knowledge and Vision
Nasafi analogizes the vision of the unseen God to knowledge of the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the analogy between knowledge and vision to defend the possibility of seeing Allah without spatial or physical conditions.

Core Idea
God's vision is not bound by the conditions of created vision.

In this passage, Nasafi addresses a criticism that knowledge of the unseen must follow the same pattern as knowledge of the seen. He counters by drawing an analogy with vision: just as knowledge of the unseen does not require sensory conditions, so too vision of God is not conditioned by distance, direction, or physical rays. This clarifies that God can be seen as He is, without spatial limitations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Nasafi uses a very compressed argumentative style.
2
Identify the objection and the analogy
Find the opponent's claim about knowledge and Nasafi's counter using vision. Understand how the analogy works: just as knowledge of the unseen doesn't replicate the conditions of witnessed knowledge, vision of God doesn't need the conditions of created vision.
The key phrase is 'قلنا : وكذا الرؤية' (we say: and similarly vision).
3
Analyze the refutation of vision conditions
Focus on the part where Nasafi dismisses distance, confrontation, and ray connection as necessary for vision. He explains that bodies in space are seen in a direction due to their nature, not because direction is essential to vision itself. God, being infinite and not in a direction, is seen without such conditions.
This refutes the misconception that divine vision requires physical proximity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا قولهم : إن العلم بالغائب لا يخرج عن الوجه الذي به يعلم الشاهد. قلنا : وكذا الرؤية فإنها كما كانت في حق الشاهد ضرورة كانت في حق الغائب كذا. وما ذكر من المسافة والمقابلة واتصال الشعاع قد أبطلنا أن يكون شيء منه من لوازم الرؤية بل كل شيء يرى كما هو وعلى ما هو، فلما كانت الأجسام في الجهات منا وبيننا وبينها قواء رأيناها كما هي وعلى ما هي عليه، لا لأن الجهة والمقابلة من لوازم الرؤية. والله تعالى ليس بمتناه ولا منا بجهة فيرى كما هو يرانا هو لا عن جهة ولا في جهة.
And similarly their statement: 'Knowledge of the unseen does not depart from the manner by which the witnessed is known.' We say: And similarly vision, for just as it is in the case of the witnessed by necessity, it is in the case of the unseen likewise. And what was mentioned of distance, confrontation, and connection of a ray, we have already refuted that any of them is a necessary concomitant of vision; rather, everything is seen as it is and as it is. Since bodies are in directions from us and between us and them there is empty space, we see them as they are and as they are, not because direction and confrontation are necessary concomitants of vision. And God the Exalted is not finite nor in a direction from us, so He is seen as He is; He sees us not from a direction nor in a direction.
Source pages p. 644

Commentary
This passage responds to the claim that knowledge of the unseen must follow the same pattern as knowledge of the witnessed. Nasafi retorts that vision is analogous: just as vision of witnessed things occurs necessarily under certain conditions (here, direction etc.), so too vision of the unseen (God) occurs in its own manner. He then reiterates that distance, confrontation, and ray connection have been proven not to be necessary for vision; rather, every object is seen as it is. Bodies in space are seen in a direction because they exist in space with empty space between, but that is due to their nature, not because direction is a necessary condition for vision itself. God, being infinite and not in a direction, is seen as He is, without direction. He also sees us without direction. Thus the analogy with knowledge supports the claim that God's vision is not conditioned by physical requirements.

Key Terms
العلم بالغائب
الشاهد
المسافة
القواء
الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that analogical reasoning about God must be unqualified—that since we see created things only in a direction, we cannot see God at all.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the analogy between knowledge and vision is valid, but the conditions (direction, distance) are not transferable to God because they are not essential to vision itself.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi argue about vision in relation to the analogy with knowledge?
A) Vision of the unseen must follow exactly the same rules as vision of the witnessed.
B) Vision of the unseen is analogous to knowledge of the unseen, not requiring same conditions.
C) Vision is only possible for the witnessed, not the unseen.
D) The analogy fails because knowledge is different from vision.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi defends the vision of God by analogy with knowledge: both do not require the conditions of the created world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you place limitations on how you think God can see you or be seen? Have you ever felt that because you cannot see God now, He is far away? How does this lesson challenge that assumption? What will you do to remind yourself that God sees you even without direction or distance?
?
When you try to know God, do you demand that His attributes conform to your physical experience? This lesson says knowledge of the unseen is not bound by the rules of the witnessed. In your daily life, do you trust God's unseen wisdom even when it contradicts your immediate senses? What area of your life do you need to let go of your own 'sensory' expectations and submit to His unseen plan?
?
The passage refutes that direction and confrontation are necessary for vision. Do you sometimes assume God is 'nearer' when you feel something, or 'farther' when you don't? How can you train yourself to know that God is always 'seen' to you through His signs, regardless of your emotional state? Write down one concrete step to act on that conviction today.

Refuting the Whole and Part Objection
Nasafi refutes the objection that if God is seen, He must be seen partially or wholly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that God must be seen wholly or partially is invalid, and how Nasafi refutes it by showing that 'whole' and 'part' only apply to composites.

Core Idea
Seeing something does not require it to be whole or part — God is seen as He is.

When considering the vision of God, some assume that if God is seen, He must be seen either wholly or partially, implying limitation. Nasafi directly refutes this assumption by showing that the notions of 'whole' and 'part' only apply to composite beings. In this passage, he presents both a dialectical argument and a precise conceptual answer, using the example of accidents to clarify.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow before you interpret.
Notice the structure: first a dialectical objection-answer, then the real answer, then an analogy.
2
Understand the dialectical argument
Nasafi counters by asking objectors whether they know God wholly or partially. Explore why each possible answer leads to impossibility or disbelief, except the answer 'we know Him as He is.'
This argument mirrors the same logic for God seeing us.
3
Grasp the real answer
Nasafi defines 'whole' as a composition of limited parts and 'part' as a component. These cannot apply to God, so He is seen as He is, not as whole or part.
Consider how this definition removes the objection.
4
Study the analogy with accidents
Nasafi uses the example of seeing a movement (an accident): an accident is not described as whole or part, yet it is seen. This reinforces that vision does not require compositeness.
The analogy extends to the indivisible atom.
5
Conclude the lesson
Reflect on the key takeaway: God is seen as He is, without whole or part, because these terms only apply to composites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قالوا: لو كان الله تعالى يرى بعضه أو كله على ما قرروا كلام فاسد دفعتهم إليه الحيرة. وأقرب ما يجابون عنه بأن يقابل بالعلم فيقال: أتعلمون الله كله أم بعضه؟ فإن قالوا عرفنا كله أو بعضه أحالوا. وإن قالوا إذا لم نعرف كله ولا بعضه لم نعرفه، كفروا. وإن قالوا نعرفه كما هو وهو ليس بموصوف بالكل والبعض فهو لهم جواب. وكذا يقال: الله تعالى يرانا، أفيرانا بعضه أم كله ؟ والكلام فيه كما في الأول. ثم حقيقة الجواب أن الكل اسم لجملة تركبت من أجزاء محصورة، والبعض اسم لكل جزء وتركب الكل منه ومن غيره، وذلك كله لا يليق بصفات الباري فلم يكن كلا ولا بعضا، فيرى كما هو واعتبر هذا بالعلم به. ثم هذا يبطل، برؤية الأعراض إذ يرى لو أن حركةً، فلو قيل: رأيت كله أم بعضه؟ فبأي الجوابين أجاب أحال، وليس له من الجواب إلا أن يقول: العرض لا يوصف بالكل والبعض لما مر من تحديدهما وذلك محال على العرض. فيرى العرض لا كله ولا بعضه بل يرى على ما هو عليه. فكذا الباري جل وعلا يرى على ما هو عليه لا كله ولا بعضه. وكذا الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ.
And what they said: 'If God the Exalted were seen, either part of Him or all of Him' – as they asserted – is corrupt speech to which confusion drove them. The closest way to answer them is to counter with knowledge and say: Do you know God wholly or partially? If they say we know Him wholly or partially, they fall into impossibility. And if they say: If we do not know Him wholly nor partially, we do not know Him, they disbelieve. And if they say: We know Him as He is, and He is not described by wholeness and partiality, that is an answer for them. And similarly it is said: God the Exalted sees us; does He see us partially or wholly? And the discussion about it is as in the first. Then the real answer is that 'whole' is a name for a composition consisting of limited parts, and 'partial' is a name for each part from which the whole is composed along with others, and all of that is not befitting the attributes of the Creator; so He is not whole nor partial, and He is seen as He is. Consider this in relation to knowledge of Him. Then this is refuted by the vision of accidents, for a movement, if seen, and if it were said: Did you see it wholly or partially? whichever answer he gives he falls into impossibility, and he has no answer except to say: The accident is not described by wholeness and partiality due to what has passed of their definitions, and that is impossible for the accident. So the accident is seen not wholly nor partially, but seen as it is. So likewise the Creator, the Mighty and Exalted, is seen as He is, not wholly nor partially. And likewise the indivisible atom.
Source pages p. 645

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that if God is seen, He must be seen either wholly or partially. Nasafi first presents a dialectical response using knowledge: ask the objector whether they know God wholly or partially. If they say wholly or partially, they imply composition (impossible); if they say they don't know Him at all, they are disbelievers; the only correct answer is that they know Him as He is, without whole/part. The same applies to God's seeing us. Then he gives the real answer: 'whole' and 'part' apply only to composites of limited parts, which does not apply to God. He reinforces this by the example of seeing an accident (like motion): an accident is not described as whole or part, yet it is seen. Thus God, not being a composite, is seen as He is, without whole or part. This also applies to the indivisible atom (al-juz' alladhi la yatajaza').

Key Terms
الكل
البعض
العرض
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God is seen, He must be seen either wholly or partially, implying He is a composite being with parts.
Correction: Vision does not require compositeness. 'Whole' and 'part' only apply to composites, and God is not a composite. He is seen as He is, without being described by whole or part, just as accidents and indivisible atoms are seen without such descriptions.

Checkpoint
What is the 'real answer' (حقيقة الجواب) to the whole/part objection?
A) God is seen wholly because He is all-encompassing.
B) 'Whole' and 'part' apply only to composites, so God is seen without them.
C) God is seen partially because He is incomprehensible.
D) The objection is invalid because we do not see God at all.

Key Takeaway
God is seen as He is, without being described by wholeness or partiality, because these terms only apply to composites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the fact that God is seen 'as He is' and not as 'whole or part' affect your longing to see Him? Are you content to see Him as He is, or do you still imagine Him in limited ways?
?
Nasafi uses the example of seeing an accident to show that vision does not require composition. In your own life, when have you tried to reduce God to your own categories? What would it mean to let go of those categories and know God as He is?
?
The objectors were confused by their own assumptions. What assumptions do you hold about God that might be limiting your understanding of Him? Are you willing to be corrected by the truth, even if it challenges your comfortable concepts?

Framing the Vision of God: Eschatological vs Essential
Nasafi distinguishes between two framings of the vision question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi recommends framing the vision of God debate in terms of essential visibility rather than eschatological occurrence.

Core Idea
Shift the question from 'when' to 'what' — focus on essential visibility to sidestep objections.

Nasafi notes that many standard objections to the vision of God apply only to those who affirm it in the afterlife. He recommends reframing the discussion: ask whether God's essence is intrinsically visible, not when it will occur. This methodological shift avoids irrelevant objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to absorb the structure.
Notice the contrast between 'من يقول' and 'من فرض الكلام'.
2
Identify the two framings
The passage presents two ways to discuss divine vision: eschatological (seen in afterlife) and essential (visible in itself). Find the Arabic phrases for each.
3
Understand the methodological advice
The Samarqand scholars advised their companions to adopt the essential framing. Ask: why does this avoid most objections?
The commentary explains that objections like the whole/part dilemma apply only to the eschatological view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أكثر هذه الأسئلة ترد على من يقول : يرى الله تعالى في الآخرة. فأما من فرض الكلام في أن ذاته هل هو مرئي في نفسه فيسقط عنه أكثر هذه الأسئلة. وبعض الكبراء المحققين من أئمتنا بسمرقند كان يوصي أصحابنا أن يفرضوا
