This passage notes that most of the preceding objections (like the whole/part dilemma) apply to those who affirm that God will be seen specifically in the afterlife (الآخرة). However, if one shifts the discussion to the essential visibility of God's essence — i.e., whether His essence is intrinsically capable of being seen (مرئي في نفسه) — then many of those objections become irrelevant. The passage then mentions that some senior verifying scholars among the Maturidi imams in Samarqand used to advise their companions to posit (probably to posit the discussion of essential visibility). The sentence is cut off, but the intent is to recommend framing the question in terms of essential visibility rather than eschatological occurrence to avoid unnecessary objections.

Answering the Whole-or-Part Objection by Analogy with Knowledge
Al-Nasafi responds to the whole-or-part objection using knowledge as an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi refutes the objection to seeing God by turning the dilemma back onto knowledge of God.

Core Idea
The same dilemma used against seeing God also applies to knowing God — and the answer is 'as He is.'

Opponents of the vision of God argue that seeing must involve either the whole or a part of God, both of which are impossible for the Divine. Al-Nasafi cleverly responds by asking whether they know God entirely or partially, exposing the same logical trap. This passage shows how a theological objection can be neutralised by reverse analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note the structure: objection, counter-question, three possible answers, and extension to God's seeing.
The passage is a continuous argument.
2
Identify the three possible responses
Al-Nasafi presents three possible answers to the question about knowledge of God. List them and note the consequence of each.
The third response is the correct one.
3
Connect the analogy
Understand how the same reasoning applied to knowledge also applies to the original objection about seeing. See why the objection is self-defeating.
This shows the power of logical refutation in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قالوا: لو كان الله تعالى يرى بعضه أو كله على ما قرروا كلام فاسد دفعتهم إليه الحيرة. وأقرب ما يجابون عنه بأن يقابل بالعلم فيقال: أتعلمون الله كله أم بعضه؟ فإن قالوا عرفنا كله أو بعضه أحالوا. وإن قالوا إذا لم نعرف كله ولا بعضه لم نعرفه، كفروا. وإن قالوا نعرفه كما هو وهو ليس بموصوف بالكل والبعض فهو لهم جواب. وكذا يقال: الله تعالى يرانا، أفيرانا بعضه أم كله؟ والكلام فيه كما في الأول.
And what they said: 'If God the Exalted sees some of Him or all of Him' according to what they established is corrupt speech to which confusion drives them. And the closest way to answer them is to counter with knowledge and say: Do you know God entirely or partially? If they say we know Him entirely or partially they fall into impossibility. If they say we do not know Him entirely nor partially and we do not know Him, they disbelieve. If they say we know Him as He is, and He is not described by whole or part, that is for them an answer. And likewise it is said: God the Exalted sees us—does He see some of us or all of us? And the discussion concerning it is as in the first.
Source pages p. 645

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection against the possibility of seeing God in the hereafter. The opponents argue that if God is seen, one must see either all of Him or a part of Him, both of which are impossible for God. Al-Nasafi responds by turning the argument around: he asks whether they know God entirely or partially. The same dilemma applies: claiming to know God entirely or partially is impossible, but denying knowledge of Him entirely or partially leads to disbelief. The only consistent answer is that they know God as He is, without being described by 'whole' or 'part.' This answer also applies to the claim that God sees us: we do not say God sees all of us or part of us, but that He sees us as we are.

Key Terms
رؤية
كل
بعض
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That seeing God must involve seeing either the whole or a part of God, making it impossible.
Correction: The same logic applies to knowing God, and the correct answer is that we know Him as He is, not by whole or part.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the closest way to answer those who object that seeing God implies whole or part?
A) By denying that God can be seen at all.
B) By countering with the same question about knowledge of God.
C) By stating that God is composed of parts.
D) By saying that God sees only part of Himself.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi refutes the objection to seeing God by showing that the same dilemma applies to knowledge of God, and the only consistent answer is to know God as He is.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
Al-Nasafi warns that denying knowledge of God leads to disbelief. Have you ever avoided deeper reflection on God because you felt overwhelmed or inadequate? This lesson calls you to press in—how will you intentionally seek to know Him more this week?

Whole and Part Do Not Apply to God
Al-Nasafi explains the true answer regarding whole/part and God's vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the terms 'whole' and 'part' cannot be used for God, and how Al-Nasafi uses the vision of accidents to demonstrate this principle.

Core Idea
God is not composed, so whole and part are inapplicable to Him.

Al-Nasafi now gives the definitive answer to the objection that seeing God would require seeing Him as whole or part. He defines these terms and shows they only apply to composite things. Then he proves his point by considering how we see accidents—like motion—without applying whole/part. This establishes that God is seen as He is, without division.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definitions
Read the passage's definitions of 'whole' and 'part'. Note that these terms apply only to composed things.
'Whole' is a totality of limited parts; 'part' is a component.
2
See the analogy with accidents
Al-Nasafi uses the example of seeing a motion (an accident). If asked whether you saw all of it or part of it, both answers are impossible. This shows that some things are seen without whole/part.
3
Apply to God and the indivisible atom
Conclude that God is seen as He is, neither whole nor part. The indivisible atom (al-juz' alladhi la yatajzza') also avoids these categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم حقيقة الجواب أن الكل اسم لجملة تركبت من أجزاء محصورة، والبعض اسم لكل جزء وتركب الكل منه ومن غيره، وذلك كله لا يليق بصفات الباري فلم يكن كلا ولا بعضا، فيرى كما هو واعتبر هذا بالعلم به. ثم هذا يبطل، برؤية الأعراض إذ يرى لو أن حركة، فلو قيل: رأيت كله أم بعضه؟ فبأي الجوابين أجاب أحال، وليس له من الجواب إلا أن يقول: العرض يوصف بالكل والبعض لما مر من تحديدهما وذلك محال على العرض. فيرى العرض لا كله ولا بعضه بل يرى على ما هو عليه. فكذا الباري جل وعلا يرى على ما هو عليه لا كله ولا بعضه. وكذا الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ.
Then the true answer is that 'whole' is a name for a totality composed of limited parts, and 'part' is a name for each part from which the whole and others are composed. All of that is not fitting for the attributes of the Creator, so He is neither whole nor part. He is seen as He is. And consider this by analogy with knowledge of Him. Then this is invalidated by the vision of accidents, since one sees, for example, a movement. If it were said: Did you see all of it or part of it? Whichever of the two answers one gives leads to impossibility. He has no answer except to say: The accident is described by whole and part due to what has passed of their definitions, and that is impossible for the accident. So one sees the accident neither as whole nor part, but sees it as it is. Likewise the Exalted and Glorious Creator is seen as He is, not whole nor part. And likewise the indivisible atom.
Source pages p. 645

Commentary
This passage gives the true resolution of the whole-or-part objection. 'Whole' and 'part' are terms that apply only to composite things. God is not composite, so these terms do not apply to Him—He is seen as He is. Al-Nasafi then proves this by considering the vision of an accident, like motion. If one asks whether one saw all of the motion or part of it, both answers are impossible because an accident is not composed in that way. The only proper answer is that one sees it as it is, not as whole or part. Similarly, the indivisible atom (al-juz' alladhi la yatajzza') is seen without being described by whole or part. Therefore, God is seen as He is, without division.

Key Terms
كل
بعض
عرض
جزء لا يتجزأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Whenever something is seen, it must be seen as either whole or part.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that accidents and the indivisible atom are seen without being described as whole or part, so God is likewise.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that 'whole' and 'part' are inapplicable to God?
A) Because God is too great to be seen.
B) Because God is not composed of parts.
C) Because the terms are only used for bodies.
D) Because the question is forbidden to ask.

Key Takeaway
Whole and part are terms for composition; God has no composition, so He is seen as He is, not as whole or part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you unintentionally imagine Him as having parts or limits? How does this passage correct that tendency, and what must you change in your mental image?
?
If you were asked to describe what you 'see' of God with your heart, would you be tempted to divide His attributes? Why is that a subtle form of shirk, and how can you train your mind to see Him as He is?
?
Al-Nasafi uses the example of seeing a motion—something you experience daily. How often do you think about the limitations of your perception? What does this teach you about trusting your intellect versus revelation when it comes to understanding God?

Answering Why God Is Not Seen Now Despite No Veil
Al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite objection using a dilemma and analogical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite argument that the present non-vision of God proves He cannot be seen, using a dilemma and analogical reasoning about the nature of obstacles to vision.

Core Idea
Non-vision now does not prove impossibility; the obstacle is in our capacity, not in God.

The Mu'tazilites argue that since we do not see God now despite there being no veil or obstacle, this proves that seeing God is impossible. Al-Nasafi responds with a clever dilemma: either the obstacle is an opposite (blindness) or external factors. He then shows that both horns lead to conclusions the Mu'tazilites cannot accept, and further argues that God can strengthen our sight, so non-vision now is due to our weakness, not God's invisibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite objection
Start by reading the opening lines where the objection is presented: why is God not seen now despite no veil? This is the challenge al-Nasafi will refute.
Notice that the objection assumes that if something is visible, it would be seen now.
2
Follow the dilemma: what is the obstacle?
Al-Nasafi presents two possible explanations for the absence of vision: either the obstacle is an opposite (like blindness) residing in the viewer, or it is external factors like veils, smallness, or distance. Identify these two horns.
This is the key structuring move of the argument.
3
Examine the first horn: opposition in the locus
If the Mu'tazilites choose that the obstacle is an opposite (i.e., blindness), then they admit that vision is only prevented by an opposite. Since there is no blindness in the believers now, God could be seen. Thus they have conceded the point.
Al-Nasafi says this cuts off their clamor.
4
Examine the second horn: external factors and implications
If they choose external factors, then vision can cease without an opposite. This leads to a further argument: if vision can cease without an opposite, then other accidents like motion and color can also cease without an opposite—something the Mu'tazilites deny. Thus this horn is also problematic.
This is an analogical reduction.
5
Consider the capacity argument
Al-Nasafi then argues that God can strengthen our eyesight to see small and distant objects. Therefore minuteness is not an absolute barrier to vision—it sometimes accompanies vision and sometimes not. Hence the current non-vision is due to our limited strength, not impossibility.
This shows that the cause of non-vision is not intrinsic to the object.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد خرج الجواب عما قالوا: إنا لماذا لا نرى الله في الحال ولا حجب ولا موانع؟ فإنا بينا قبل ذلك ما هو جوابه على الاستقصاء، ثم نزيد لهذا إيضاحا لأن المعتزلة يتشبثون به ويوهمون الأحداث أنه من قبيل ما لا انفصال عنه. فنقول لهم: وجود الحجب والسواتر ودقة المرئي وبعده ما المانع من الرؤية أقيام ضدها بمحلها أم الحجب والسواتر والدقة واللطافة والبعد؟ فإن قالوا بالأول، فقد أذعنوا للحق وانقطع شغبهم، وإن قالوا بالثاني وأضافوا ارتفاع الرؤية إلى هذه الأشياء وإن لم تكن قائمة بمحل الرؤية. قيل لهم: إذا جاز أن تنعدم الرؤية للشيء لا إلى ضدها فلم لا يجوز ذلك في الأعراض حتى يخلو الجسم عن الحركة لا إلى ضدها ومن اللون لا إلى ضد له. ثم يقال لهم: أليس قد يجوز أن يقوى الله تعالى أبصارنا فنرى الجسم الصغير والدقيق والبعيد؟ فإذا قالوا نعم. قيل لهم: فما أنكرتم من أن تكون الدقة التي تجامع الرؤية تارة ولا تجامعها تارة لا تكون علة لأن يكون الشيء غير مرئي كما لا تكون علة لأن يكون مرئيا إذ قد تجامع الرؤية تارة ولا تجامعها تارة. ويقال لهم: ما أنكرتم أن يكون ما يجامع الرؤية تارة ولا يجامعها تارة لا يكون علة لأن كان الشيء مرئيا كما أن وجود الجسم الذي يجامع الحركة تارة ولا يجامعها تارة لا يكون علة لأن كان الشيء متحركا. وكذا لا يكون علة لئلا يكون مرئيا لوجود العلة في
And the answer has emerged to what they said: 'Why do we not see God at present, while there is no veil nor obstacle?' For we have already explained before its answer in detail. Then we add clarification to this because the Mu'tazilites cling to it and make the common people imagine that it is of the kind from which there is no escape. So we say to them: The existence of veils, screens, minuteness of the seen object, and its distance—what is the obstacle to vision? Is it the existence of its opposite in its locus, or are the veils, screens, minuteness, subtlety, and distance? If they say the first, they have acknowledged the truth and their clamor is cut off. If they say the second and attribute the cessation of vision to these things even though they are not subsisting in the locus of vision, it is said to them: If it is permissible for vision of a thing to cease without [arising from] its opposite, then why is that not permissible for accidents, so that a body becomes devoid of motion without an opposite, and of color without an opposite? Then it is said to them: Is it not possible that God the Exalted strengthens our eyesight so that we see a small, minute, and distant body? If they say yes, it is said to them: What prevents you from [accepting] that minuteness, which sometimes accompanies vision and sometimes does not accompany it, is not a cause for a thing being unseen, just as it is not a cause for it being seen, since it sometimes accompanies vision and sometimes does not? And it is said to them: What prevents you from [accepting] that what accompanies vision sometimes and does not accompany it sometimes is not a cause for a thing being visible, just as the existence of a body, which accompanies motion sometimes and does not accompany it sometimes, is not a cause for a thing being in motion? And likewise it is not a cause for it being unseen, because of the existence of the cause in
Source pages p. 646

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed refutation of a Mu'tazilite objection: why is God not seen now if there is no veil or obstacle? The author says he has already answered this before but adds further clarity. He presents a two-part dilemma: the obstacle to vision is either the existence of an opposite in the locus of vision (i.e., blindness) or external factors like veils, distance, etc. If they say the first, they admit that vision is only prevented by an opposite, which is not currently the case, so God could be seen. If they say the second, then vision can cease without an opposite—and the author uses this to argue by analogy: if vision can cease without an opposite, then other things like motion and color can also cease without an opposite, which the Mu'tazilites deny. Then he argues that God can strengthen our eyesight so that we see small and distant objects. If they accept that, then minuteness is not an absolute barrier to vision—it sometimes accompanies vision, sometimes not—so it cannot be a cause for something being unseen. Similarly, bodily constitution accompanies motion contingently, not as a cause. The argument is cut off but aims to show that the absence of vision now does not imply impossibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God were visible, we would see Him now, since there is no veil.
Correction: Non-vision now is due to our limited capacity and weakness, not God's invisibility. God can strengthen our sight in the Hereafter, making vision possible.

Checkpoint

A) By arguing that God is not visible at all.
B) By showing that vision can cease without an opposite and that God can strengthen sight.
C) By accepting that veils are necessary for vision.
D) By denying that God could be seen even in the hereafter.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite objection by showing that the absence of vision now does not prove impossibility, because the obstacle could be our weakness, not God's nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel spiritually distant and wonder why God does not make His presence more obvious to you, do you tend to blame Him for hiding or yourself for not being ready? How does Al-Nasafi's argument challenge you to take responsibility for cultivating the spiritual eyesight to see God's signs now, and to long for the ultimate vision in the hereafter?
?
The Mu'tazilites demanded immediate evidence. Have you ever caught yourself demanding that God prove Himself to you on your terms, rather than trusting His wisdom in delaying full disclosure? What would it mean for you to accept that your current inability to see God is a reflection of your own limitation, not His deficiency, and to humbly ask Him to strengthen your faith?
?
Al-Nasafi notes that the same minuteness sometimes allows vision and sometimes blocks it, depending on the strength of the sight. What are the 'minute' distractions in your life—small sins or worldly attachments—that sometimes cloud your perception of God and sometimes don't? What concrete step will you take today to remove a particular barrier that you have been tolerating?

The Initial Challenge: Why Do We Not See God Now?
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila objection and the first bifurcation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's objection that God should be visible now and the first key distinction between causes of non-vision (opposite vs external factors).

Core Idea
Non-vision is either due to its opposite or external factors—neither supports the Mu'tazila.

The Mu'tazila argue that since there are no veils or obstacles, we should see God now. Imam Nasafi begins his refutation by analyzing the possible causes for the absence of vision. He presents a crucial bifurcation that will undermine their entire case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Focus on the question and the two possible answers given by Nasafi.
The passage is dense; do not rush.
2
Identify the bifurcation
Find the two possibilities Nasafi presents for why vision is absent: either because of an opposite in the locus, or because of external factors like veils, smallness, distance.
This is the core logical move.
3
Understand the implications
Consider what follows if the Mu'tazila choose either option. If they choose 'opposite', they concede the principle; if they choose 'external', the next arguments in later lessons will show inconsistency.
This sets the stage for the entire refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبصرة الأدلة لأبي المعين النسفي ج١
الكلام في إثبات كونه في ذاته مرئيا لتندفع أكثر هذه الأسئلة.
وقد خرج الجواب عما قالوا: إنا لماذا لا نرى الله في الحال ولا حجب ولا موانع؟ فإنا بينا قبل ذلك ما هو جوابه على الاستقصاء، ثم نزيد لهذا إيضاحا لأن المعتزلة يتشبثون به ويوهمون الأحداث (١) أنه من قبيل ما لا انفصال عنه. فنقول لهم: وجود الحجب والسواتر ودقة المرئي وبعده ما المانع من الرؤية أقيام ضدها بمحلها أم الحجب والسواتر والدقة واللطافة والبعد ؟ فإن قالوا بالأول، فقد أذعنوا للحق وانقطع شغبهم، وإن قالوا بالثاني وأضافوا ارتفاع الرؤية إلى هذه الأشياء وإن لم تكن قائمة بمحل الرؤية.
جميع
قيل لهم : إذا جاز أن تنعدم الرؤية للشيء لا إلى ضدها فلم لا يجوز ذلك في الأعراض حتى يخلو الجسم عن الحركة لا إلى ضدها ومن اللون لا إلى ضد له. ثم يقال لهم : أليس قد يجوز أن يقوى الله تعالى أبصارنا فنرى الجسم الصغير والدقيق والبعيد؟
فإذا قالوا نعم. قيل لهم: فما أنكرتم من أن تكون الدقة التي تجامع الرؤية(٢) تارة ولا تجامعها تارة لا تكون علة لأن يكون الشيء غير مرئي كما لا تكون علة لأن يكون مرئيا إذ (٣) قد تجامع الرؤية تارة ولا تجامعها تارة. ويقال لهم: ما أنكرتم أن يكون ما يجامع الرؤية تارة ولا يجامعها تارة لا يكون علة لأن كان الشيء مرئيا كما أن وجود (٤) الجسم الذي يجامع الحركة تارة ولا يجامعها تارة لا يكون علة لأن كان الشيء متحركا. وكذا لا يكون علة لئلا يكون مرئيا لوجود العلة في
الفصلين ولا معلول
ويقال : كيف تنتفي رؤية الدقيق والبعيد من العين لا بضد جعل محل الرؤية الحالة في عين الرائي والغيبة الحالة في غيره؟ وكيف يجوز أن ينتفي الشيء بوجود عرض في غير محله؟
ثم يقال لهم (١): ولم زعمتم أن ما يجوز أن يرى إذ لم نره (فإنما لم نره)(٢) للخلال ) (٣) التي عددتموها من الدقة والحجاب والغيبة والبعد واللطافة؟ فإن ادعوا أن فيما بينا كذلك.
قيل لهم: ولم زعمتم أن ذلك فيما بينا كذلك مع مخالفتنا إياكم فيما ادعيتموه ومنعنا أن يكون شيء من ذلك لا يرى كما ذكرتم في الشاهد وإحالتنا انعدام الرؤية إلى وجود ضد الرؤية.
ثم يقال لهم : لم يزل رأيكم الرجوع إلى مجرد الوجود والعمي عن حقائق العلل ولم قضيتم بذلك إذا وجدتم كذلك ثم يطالبون بأن يقضوا أن الشيء في الغائب لا يكون إلا جوهرا أو عرضا والقائم بنفسه لا يكون إلا جوهراً) (٤) لوجودهم كذلك، ثم من غباوتهم أنهم وجدوا في الشاهد كل عالم موصوفا بالعلم ثم لم يقضوا بذلك في الغائب، وإن تعلق العلم بالعالم تعلق العلل بالمعلولات ثم قضوا في الغائب بما اقترن بآخر في الشاهد اقتران الوجود، وهذا مما لا يخفى فساده.
ولما رأي بعض أصحاب الجبائي هذا الإلزام زعم أن دقة الجسم مانعة من الرؤية من أجل ضعف في البصر (٥) . فيقال لهم : أفيجوز ارتفاع الضعف مع وجود الدقة ؟ فإن قالوا: لا، أحالوا في كلامهم، لأن قيام معنى في محل لا يمنع من وجود
Tabsirat al-Adilla of Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, vol. 1. Discourse on proving that He is visible in His essence to repulse most of these questions. And we have given the answer to what they said: Why do we not see God now, without veils or obstacles? For we explained before what its answer is in detail, then we add clarification to this because the Mu'tazila cling to it and make the common people think (1) that it is of the kind from which there is no separation. So we say to them: The existence of veils, screens, smallness of the seen object, and its distance—what prevents vision? Is it the existence of its opposite in its locus, or the veils, screens, smallness, subtlety, and distance? If they say the first, they have submitted to the truth and their clamor ceases. If they say the second and attribute the absence of vision to these things even though they are not subsisting in the locus of vision—all of them—it is said to them: If it is permissible that vision ceases for a thing without its opposite, then why is that not permissible for accidents so that the body becomes devoid of motion without its opposite, and of color without its opposite? Then it is said to them: Is it not possible that Allah Almighty strengthens our sight so that we see the small, minute, and distant body? If they say yes, it is said to them: What prevents you from [accepting] that the smallness which accompanies vision sometimes and does not accompany it other times is not a cause for the thing to be unseen, just as it is not a cause for it to be seen, since (3) it may accompany vision sometimes and not accompany it other times. And it is said to them: What prevents you from [accepting] that what accompanies vision sometimes and does not accompany it other times is not a cause for the thing to be seen, just as the existence (4) of the body which accompanies motion sometimes and does not accompany it other times is not a cause for the thing to be moving. Similarly, it is not a cause for it not to be seen because the cause exists in both states and there is no effect. And it is said: How does the vision of the minute and distant cease from the eye without an opposite that makes the locus of vision [reside] in the eye of the seer and the absence reside in another? And how is it permissible that a thing ceases due to the existence of an accident in something other than its locus? Then it is said to them (1): Why do you claim that what is possible to be seen, when we do not see it, (we do not see it only)(2) due to the defects (3) that you enumerated: smallness, veil, absence, distance, and subtlety? If they claim that what is between us is like that, it is said to them: Why do you claim that what is between us is like that, despite our disagreement with you regarding what you have claimed, and our denial that any of that is unseen as you mentioned in the witnessed world, and our assertion that the cessation of vision is due to the existence of the opposite of vision? Then it is said to them: Your opinion always reverts to mere existence and blindness to the realities of causes. And why did you judge according to that when you found it so? Then they are required to judge that the thing in the unseen is not except substance or accident, and that which subsists by itself is not except substance (4) because they found it so. Then, from their foolishness, they found in the witnessed world every knower described with knowledge, yet they did not judge accordingly in the unseen; and although knowledge is related to the knower as causes are related to effects, they judged in the unseen according to what is conjoined with another in the witnessed world by conjunction of existence. And this, its corruption is not hidden. And when one of the disciples of al-Jubba'i saw this implication, he claimed that the smallness of the body prevents vision because of weakness in sight (5). So it is said to them: Is it possible that weakness disappears while smallness exists? If they say no, they have made an impossibility in their speech, because the subsistence of a meaning in a locus does not prevent the existence of
Source pages p. 646

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Mu'tazila objections regarding the impossibility of seeing God (ru'ya) in this world. The Imam explains that he has already given a detailed answer but adds clarification because the Mu'tazila mislead common people by claiming that the impossibility of seeing God now is an inherent impossibility. He then presents a series of dialectical arguments. First, he asks the Mu'tazila: what prevents vision now? Is it the existence of an opposite (like blindness) in the locus of vision, or is it external factors like veils, smallness, distance? If they admit it is due to an opposite, they concede the principle that absence of vision requires an opposite, which would then apply in the case of God (who has no opposite), thus making ru'ya possible if He wills. If they say it is due to external factors, then the Imam argues that if vision can cease without an opposite, why cannot accidents like motion and color cease without their opposites? This shows inconsistency. Next, he questions whether Allah can strengthen our sight to see small and distant objects. If they agree, then smallness is not a permanent cause of invisibility because it sometimes accompanies vision (when sight is strong) and sometimes not. Thus, smallness is not a true cause (ʿilla) of invisibility. Similarly, he draws an analogy: the existence of a body sometimes accompanies motion and sometimes not, so it is not a cause of motion; likewise, external factors are not causes of invisibility. He then asks: how can vision of a minute or distant object be negated by an accident (like smallness) that does not reside in the eye itself? This is impossible because effects require their causes to be in the same locus. Then he challenges their claim that we do not see God only due to obstacles like smallness, veil, etc. But the Imam denies that these are true obstacles based on the witnessed world (shāhid), and asserts that the true cause of non-vision is the opposite of vision (i.e., blindness). He criticizes the Mu'tazila for inconsistently applying analogical reasoning (qiyās): they sometimes base judgments on mere coexistence in the witnessed world without establishing causality, and other times they impose strict categories (e.g., that the unseen must be either substance or accident) based on witness. But then they fail to apply the same reasoning to attributes like knowledge: in witness, every knower has knowledge, yet they do not affirm knowledge for God in the unseen, even though knowledge relates to the knower as cause to effect. This shows their methodology is flawed. Finally, he addresses a specific argument from a follower of al-Jubba'i who claimed that a body's smallness prevents vision due to weakness of sight. The Imam counters: if weakness can disappear while smallness remains, then smallness alone is not a preventer. If they say weakness cannot disappear with smallness, they contradict themselves because a meaning (maʿnā) in a locus does not preclude the existence of another meaning, so the two can coexist.

Key Terms
رؤية
ضد
حجاب
دقة
بعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that since we do not see God now, it is impossible to see Him at all.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the absence of vision now can be explained by external factors (which are not permanent) or by an opposite (which does not apply to God). Neither implies impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Nasafi's first argument, what are the two possible causes for not seeing something?
A) The existence of an opposite in the locus of vision, or external factors like veils and smallness.
B) The weakness of the seer's sight, or the absence of light.
C) The object being too small, or the seer being distant.
D) The will of God, or the sinfulness of the seer.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila are forced to admit that non-vision is due to an opposite (which does not apply to God) or external factors (which are not permanent causes).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel spiritually blind or distant from God, do you immediately assume that God is unreachable, or do you honestly ask yourself whether it is your own inner opposite (your sins, your heedlessness) or external veils (distractions, attachments) that are blocking His presence? Will you take one concrete step today to remove one of those veils?
?
The Mu'tazila used the fact that we don't see God now to deny the possibility of seeing Him in the afterlife. Have you ever allowed a temporary spiritual emptiness to make you doubt an eternal promise? How does Nasafi's logical bifurcation help you stand firm when you cannot feel God?
?
You have read that non-vision can be due to an opposite within you or external factors. Which one do you think is the greater obstacle in your own life: your internal state (like pride or laziness) or external circumstances (like busyness or bad company)? Decide right now: what will you do to address that obstacle starting this week?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Accidents and the Inconsistency of Causes
Nasafi argues that non-vision without opposite leads to absurdity with accidents, and smallness is not a true cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that if vision can cease without an opposite, then accidents like motion should also be able to cease without opposites, and that external factors are not true causes.

Core Idea
External factors like smallness are not consistent causes—they accompany vision sometimes, not always.

Nasafi now turns to the second branch. He shows that if the Mu'tazila claim vision ceases without an opposite, then why not also allow accidents like motion to cease without their opposites? He then argues that smallness and other factors cannot be true causes because they sometimes coexist with vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic translation noting the two sub-arguments: the 'accident' analogy and the 'smallness' argument with strengthened sight.
Pay attention to the word 'علة' (cause) and the analogies.
2
Analyze the accident analogy
Nasafi draws a parallel: if vision can end without its opposite (e.g., blindness), then why can't motion end without its opposite (rest)? This exposes inconsistency in Mu'tazila logic.
3
Understand the smallness argument
Nasafi asks whether Allah can strengthen sight so we see small objects. If yes, then smallness is not a permanent preventer—it is not a true cause of invisibility.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Mu'tazila objections regarding the impossibility of seeing God (ru'ya) in this world. The Imam explains that he has already given a detailed answer but adds clarification because the Mu'tazila mislead common people by claiming that the impossibility of seeing God now is an inherent impossibility. He then presents a series of dialectical arguments. First, he asks the Mu'tazila: what prevents vision now? Is it the existence of an opposite (like blindness) in the locus of vision, or is it external factors like veils, smallness, distance? If they admit it is due to an opposite, they concede the principle that absence of vision requires an opposite, which would then apply in the case of God (who has no opposite), thus making ru'ya possible if He wills. If they say it is due to external factors, then the Imam argues that if vision can cease without an opposite, why cannot accidents like motion and color cease without their opposites? This shows inconsistency. Next, he questions whether Allah can strengthen our sight to see small and distant objects. If they agree, then smallness is not a permanent cause of invisibility because it sometimes accompanies vision (when sight is strong) and sometimes not. Thus, smallness is not a true cause (ʿilla) of invisibility. Similarly, he draws an analogy: the existence of a body sometimes accompanies motion and sometimes not, so it is not a cause of motion; likewise, external factors are not causes of invisibility. He then asks: how can vision of a minute or distant object be negated by an accident (like smallness) that does not reside in the eye itself? This is impossible because effects require their causes to be in the same locus. Then he challenges their claim that we do not see God only due to obstacles like smallness, veil, etc. But the Imam denies that these are true obstacles based on the witnessed world (shāhid), and asserts that the true cause of non-vision is the opposite of vision (i.e., blindness). He criticizes the Mu'tazila for inconsistently applying analogical reasoning (qiyās): they sometimes base judgments on mere coexistence in the witnessed world without establishing causality, and other times they impose strict categories (e.g., that the unseen must be either substance or accident) based on witness. But then they fail to apply the same reasoning to attributes like knowledge: in witness, every knower has knowledge, yet they do not affirm knowledge for God in the unseen, even though knowledge relates to the knower as cause to effect. This shows their methodology is flawed. Finally, he addresses a specific argument from a follower of al-Jubba'i who claimed that a body's smallness prevents vision due to weakness of sight. The Imam counters: if weakness can disappear while smallness remains, then smallness alone is not a preventer. If they say weakness cannot disappear with smallness, they contradict themselves because a meaning (maʿnā) in a locus does not preclude the existence of another meaning, so the two can coexist.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mu'tazila think that smallness of an object is an inherent cause of its invisibility.
Correction: Nasafi shows that smallness is not a cause because it sometimes accompanies vision (when sight is strong) and sometimes not, so it is not a constant cause.

Checkpoint
How does Nasafi undermine the claim that smallness is a cause of invisibility?
A) By arguing that smallness can be overcome by strengthening sight, so it is not a constant cause.
B) By stating that smallness is an accident that cannot exist in the eye.
C) By claiming that smallness is opposite to vision.
D) By saying that smallness only applies to material objects, not to God.

Key Takeaway
External factors like smallness are not true causes because they do not produce their effect consistently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own limitations—like a weak skill or a physical shortcoming—do you assume that Allah cannot overcome it? Have you ever limited God's power by thinking 'this is just how I am'? This lesson shows that smallness is not a true barrier—nothing is outside Allah's power. What 'smallness' in your life have you accepted as final, and how will you ask Allah to strengthen your sight today?
?
The Mu'tazila assumed that because they never saw a small object without strong sight, it was impossible. Where in your life have you mistaken 'I've never seen it happen' for 'it cannot happen'? Are there spiritual or practical changes you have dismissed because they seem beyond your nature? Write down one specific area where you will stop assuming impossibility and start hoping in Allah's power to change it.
?
If smallness sometimes accompanies vision and sometimes does not, then it is not a cause. This demands that you examine the 'causes' you rely on: your health, wealth, relationships. Do you treat them as guarantees? Or do you see them as mere instruments that Allah can disconnect from their effects at any moment? Before you sleep tonight, name one outcome you are clinging to because of its 'cause', and surrender it to the One who is the only true Cause.

Methodological Critique and Final Refutations
Nasafi provides arguments on locus, methodology, and refutes a specific Mu'tazila claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's critique of Mu'tazila methodology regarding locus of causes, their inconsistent use of qiyas, and the refutation of al-Jubba'i's disciple.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila are inconsistent in applying analogy from the witnessed to the unseen world.

Nasafi continues with a series of incisive criticisms. He questions how an accident outside the eye can cause non-vision, challenges the Mu'tazila's claim about obstacles, exposes their inconsistent use of qiyas (analogical reasoning) between shahid and ghaib, and finally refutes a disciple of al-Jubba'i who proposed a different explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the translation and note the multiple objections: the locus argument, the challenge on obstacles, the methodological critique, and the final point about al-Jubba'i's disciple.
2
Understand the locus argument
Nasafi asks how an accident (like smallness) that exists in the object, not in the eye, can cause non-vision in the eye. Causes must be in the same locus as their effects.
3
Analyze the methodological critique
Nasafi points out that the Mu'tazila sometimes rely on mere coexistence in the shahid to judge the ghaib, but other times they ignore clear examples like knowledge in every knower. This inconsistency invalidates their reasoning.
4
Examine the refutation of al-Jubba'i's disciple
A follower claimed that smallness prevents vision because of weakness in sight. Nasafi counters: if weakness disappears while smallness remains, then smallness alone is not a preventer. If they say weakness cannot disappear, that is arbitrary.

And we have given the answer to what they said: Why do we not see God now, without veils or obstacles? For we explained before what its answer is in detail, then we add clarification to this because the Mu'tazila cling to it and make the common people think (1) that it is of the kind from which there is no separation. So we say to them: The existence of veils, screens, smallness of the seen object, and its distance—what prevents vision? Is it the existence of its opposite in its locus, or the veils, screens, smallness, subtlety, and distance? If they say the first, they have submitted to the truth and their clamor ceases. If they say the second and attribute the absence of vision to these things even though they are not subsisting in the locus of vision—all of them—it is said to them: If it is permissible that vision ceases for a thing without its opposite, then why is that not permissible for accidents so that the body becomes devoid of motion without its opposite, and of color without its opposite? Then it is said to them: Is it not possible that Allah Almighty strengthens our sight so that we see the small, minute, and distant body? If they say yes, it is said to them: What prevents you from [accepting] that the smallness which accompanies vision sometimes and does not accompany it other times is not a cause for the thing to be unseen, just as it is not a cause for it to be seen, since (3) it may accompany vision sometimes and not accompany it other times. And it is said to them: What prevents you from [accepting] that what accompanies vision sometimes and does not accompany it other times is not a cause for the thing to be seen, just as the existence (4) of the body which accompanies motion sometimes and does not accompany it other times is not a cause for the thing to be moving. Similarly, it is not a cause for it not to be seen because the cause exists in both states and there is no effect. And it is said: How does the vision of the minute and distant cease from the eye without an opposite that makes the locus of vision [reside] in the eye of the seer and the absence reside in another? And how is it permissible that a thing ceases due to the existence of an accident in something other than its locus? Then it is said to them (1): Why do you claim that what is possible to be seen, when we do not see it, (we do not see it only)(2) due to the defects (3) that you enumerated: smallness, veil, absence, distance, and subtlety? If they claim that what is between us is like that, it is said to them: Why do you claim that what is between us is like that, despite our disagreement with you regarding what you have claimed, and our denial that any of that is unseen as you mentioned in the witnessed world, and our assertion that the cessation of vision is due to the existence of the opposite of vision? Then it is said to them: Your opinion always reverts to mere existence and blindness to the realities of causes. And why did you judge according to that when you found it so? Then they are required to judge that the thing in the unseen is not except substance or accident, and that which subsists by itself is not except substance (4) because they found it so. Then, from their foolishness, they found in the witnessed world every knower described with knowledge, yet they did not judge accordingly in the unseen; and although knowledge is related to the knower as causes are related to effects, they judged in the unseen according to what is conjoined with another in the witnessed world by conjunction of existence. And this, its corruption is not hidden. And when one of the disciples of al-Jubba'i saw this implication, he claimed that the smallness of the body prevents vision because of weakness in sight (5). So it is said to them: Is it possible that weakness disappears while smallness exists? If they say no, they have made an impossibility in their speech, because the subsistence of a meaning in a locus does not prevent the existence of

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Mu'tazila objections regarding the impossibility of seeing God (ru'ya) in this world. The Imam explains that he has already given a detailed answer but adds clarification because the Mu'tazila mislead common people by claiming that the impossibility of seeing God now is an inherent impossibility. He then presents a series of dialectical arguments. First, he asks the Mu'tazila: what prevents vision now? Is it the existence of an opposite (like blindness) in the locus of vision, or is it external factors like veils, smallness, distance? If they admit it is due to an opposite, they concede the principle that absence of vision requires an opposite, which would then apply in the case of God (who has no opposite), thus making ru'ya possible if He wills. If they say it is due to external factors, then the Imam argues that if vision can cease without an opposite, why cannot accidents like motion and color cease without their opposites? This shows inconsistency. Next, he questions whether Allah can strengthen our sight to see small and distant objects. If they agree, then smallness is not a permanent cause of invisibility because it sometimes accompanies vision (when sight is strong) and sometimes not. Thus, smallness is not a true cause (ʿilla) of invisibility. Similarly, he draws an analogy: the existence of a body sometimes accompanies motion and sometimes not, so it is not a cause of motion; likewise, external factors are not causes of invisibility. He then asks: how can vision of a minute or distant object be negated by an accident (like smallness) that does not reside in the eye itself? This is impossible because effects require their causes to be in the same locus. Then he challenges their claim that we do not see God only due to obstacles like smallness, veil, etc. But the Imam denies that these are true obstacles based on the witnessed world (shāhid), and asserts that the true cause of non-vision is the opposite of vision (i.e., blindness). He criticizes the Mu'tazila for inconsistently applying analogical reasoning (qiyās): they sometimes base judgments on mere coexistence in the witnessed world without establishing causality, and other times they impose strict categories (e.g., that the unseen must be either substance or accident) based on witness. But then they fail to apply the same reasoning to attributes like knowledge: in witness, every knower has knowledge, yet they do not affirm knowledge for God in the unseen, even though knowledge relates to the knower as cause to effect. This shows their methodology is flawed. Finally, he addresses a specific argument from a follower of al-Jubba'i who claimed that a body's smallness prevents vision due to weakness of sight. The Imam counters: if weakness can disappear while smallness remains, then smallness alone is not a preventer. If they say weakness cannot disappear with smallness, they contradict themselves because a meaning (maʿnā) in a locus does not preclude the existence of another meaning, so the two can coexist.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some Mu'tazila think that obstacles like smallness and distance are the only reasons we do not see God, and that these are inherent causes.
Correction: Nasafi shows that these obstacles are not consistent causes, and furthermore, they cannot cause non-vision because they are not in the locus of vision. The true cause is the opposite of vision.

Checkpoint
What methodological flaw does Nasafi highlight in the Mu'tazila's reasoning?
A) They rely on Aristotelian logic.
B) They inconsistently apply qiyas: sometimes requiring strict categories, other times ignoring necessary connections like knowledge in every knower.
C) They deny the existence of the unseen world.
D) They reject all analogies from the shahid.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's methodology is inconsistent and fails to recognize true causality, especially in the case of God's visibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila were confident in their reasoning, yet blind to their own contradictions. In what area of your life do you feel most convinced you are right, yet might be overlooking a similar inconsistency? Will you examine that today, even if uncomfortable?
?
Nasafi accuses the Mu'tazila of reverting to 'mere existence' and ignoring true causes. Where in your own belief or conduct do you settle for surface-level explanations instead of seeking the deeper cause? Commit to finding one root cause this week.
?
The disciple of al-Jubba'i made an excuse that placed the blame outside himself (weakness from smallness). When you fail to fulfill a duty, do you blame external circumstances rather than the internal state of your heart? Repent and ask Allah to show you the real obstacle within you.

Refutation of the Mu'tazili Analogy for Divine Vision
Nasafi poses a rhetorical question and challenges the Mu'tazili list of physical causes for non-vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that physical obstacles to vision in the created world do not apply to seeing God, and how he exposes the logical flaw in the Mu'tazili extrapolation.

Core Idea
What prevents vision in creation may not apply to the Creator.

The Mu'tazila claimed that since small, distant, or veiled objects cannot be seen in this world, God—who is beyond such attributes—cannot be seen. Nasafi begins by questioning the very basis of this analogy, pointing out that the negation of vision is due to an opposite state in the seer, not attributes in the object.

Guided Path
1
Read the rhetorical question
Read the first two sentences where Nasafi asks how the negation of vision of a fine or distant object can be due to those attributes, when vision is negated by an opposite state in the eye.
Notice the emphasis on the locus of the cause.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili list of causes
The Mu'tazila claim that fineness, veil, absence, distance, and subtlety prevent vision. List these causes and see how Nasafi challenges each.
3
Understand the core challenge
Nasafi asks why they assume these same causes apply to the unseen. He states that he (the Maturidi) denies that those things prevent vision as they claim, and he instead attributes non-vision to the existence of the opposite of vision.
This is the key: the real cause is the opposite of vision, not physical attributes.

ويقال : كيف تنتفي رؤية الدقيق والبعيد من العين لا بضد جعل محل الرؤية الحالة في عين الرائي والغيبة الحالة في غيره؟ وكيف يجوز أن ينتفي شيء بوجود عرض في غير محله؟ ثم يقال لهم: ولم زعمتم أن ما يجوز أن يرى إذ لم نره فإنما لم نره للخلال التي عددتموها من الدقة والحجاب والغيبة والبعد واللطافة؟ فإن ادعوا أن فيما بينا كذلك. قيل لهم: ولم زعمتم أن ذلك فيما بينا كذلك مع مخالفتنا إياكم فيما ادعيتموه ومنعنا أن يكون شيء من ذلك لا يرى كما ذكرتم في الشاهد وإحالتنا انعدام الرؤية إلى وجود ضد الرؤية. ثم يقال لهم: لم يزل رأيكم الرجوع إلى مجرد الوجود والعمي عن حقائق العلل ولم قضيتم بذلك إذا وجدتم كذلك ثم يطالبون بأن يقضوا أن الشيء في الغائب لا يكون إلا جوهرا أو عرضا والقائم بنفسه لا يكون إلا جوهراً لوجودهم كذلك، ثم من غباوتهم أنهم وجدوا في الشاهد كل عالم موصوفا بالعلم ثم لم يقضوا بذلك في الغائب، وإن تعلق العلم بالعالم تعلق العلل بالمعلولات ثم قضوا في الغائب بما اقترن بآخر في الشاهد اقتران الوجود، وهذا مما لا يخفى فساده. ولما رأى بعض أصحاب الجبائي هذا الإلزام زعم أن دقة الجسم مانعة من الرؤية من أجل ضعف في البصر. فيقال لهم: أفيجوز ارتفاع الضعف مع وجود الدقة ؟ فإن قالوا: لا، أحالوا في كلامهم، لأن قيام معنى في محل لا يمنع من وجود عرض آخر في محل آخر. وإن قالوا نعم. قيل: إذا ارتفع الضعف هل نرى؟ فإن قالوا: لا، قيل لم وقد زال المانع؟ وإن قالوا: نعم، فقد رجعوا إلى الحق وأثبتوا الرؤية مع وجود الدقة فالانعدام حيث تنعدم لا يكون مضافا إلى الدقة بل على وجود ضد الرؤية، والله الموفق.
And it is said: How is vision of the fine and distant negated from the eye not by an opposite that makes the locus of vision the state in the eye of the seer and absence the state in another? And how can it be permissible that something be negated by the existence of an accident in a place other than its own? Then it is said to them: And why do you claim that what may be seen, when we have not seen it, we have not seen it only due to the defects you enumerated of fineness, veil, absence, distance, and subtlety? If they claim that in what we have explained it is so. It is said to them: And why do you claim that in what we have explained it is so, despite our opposition to you in what you have claimed and our denial that any of that is not seen as you mentioned in the witnessed world and our turning the non-existence of vision to the existence of the opposite of vision? Then it is said to them: Your opinion has not ceased to return to mere existence and blindness to the realities of causes, and why did you judge that when you found it so? Then they are demanded to judge that the thing in the unseen is nothing but substance or accident, and that which subsists by itself is nothing but substance, because they found it so. Then from their stupidity is that they found in the witnessed world every knower described by knowledge, then they did not judge that in the unseen, even though the attachment of knowledge to the knower is the attachment of causes to effects. Then they judged in the unseen by what was conjoined with another in the witnessed world with the conjunction of existence, and this is not hidden in its corruption. And when some of the followers of al-Jubba'i saw this obligating argument, he claimed that the fineness of the body prevents vision because of weakness in the eyesight. So it is said to them: Is it possible for weakness to be removed while fineness remains? If they say: No, they have made an impossibility in their speech, because the subsistence of a meaning in a locus does not prevent the existence of another accident in another locus. And if they say: Yes. It is said: If weakness is removed, do we see? If they say: No, it is said: Why, when the obstacle has been removed? And if they say: Yes, then they have returned to the truth and affirmed vision with the existence of fineness. So non-existence where it is non-existent is not attributed to fineness but to the existence of the opposite of vision, and God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 647

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazili argument that physical attributes such as fineness (smallness), veil, absence, distance, and subtlety prevent the vision of God. The author begins by posing a rhetorical question: how can the negation of vision be due to these attributes when vision is negated by an opposite state (like blindness) residing in the seer, not by an attribute in the object? He then challenges the Mu'tazila for assuming that the same causes that prevent vision in the witnessed world (e.g., smallness) apply to the unseen world (God). He points out their inconsistency: in the witnessed world, they judge that everything that exists is either substance or accident, but they do not apply this uniformly to the unseen. Specifically, they accept that a knower must have knowledge in the witnessed world, yet they deny that God (the Knower) must have knowledge in the unseen. The author then addresses a particular claim by a follower of al-Jubba'i that fineness prevents vision due to weakness in the eyesight. He asks whether weakness can be removed while fineness remains. If they say no, they contradict themselves because the existence of one attribute does not prevent another. If they say yes, then after weakness is removed, would we see? If they say no, the obstacle is removed so why not? If they say yes, they admit that fineness does not prevent vision and that the real cause is the opposite of vision (e.g., blindness). Thus the author concludes that the non-existence of vision is due to the presence of its opposite, not to the attributes they listed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Physical attributes like smallness or distance are universal causes for non-vision.
Correction: The true cause of non-vision is the opposite of vision (ضد الرؤية) in the seer, not attributes of the object.

Checkpoint

C) The existence of the opposite of vision in the seer

Key Takeaway
The negation of vision is due to the opposite of vision in the seer, not to physical attributes of the object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you struggle to see Allah's signs in your life, do you blame the 'subtlety' of the signs, or do you examine your own heart for the presence of an opposite—like arrogance or heedlessness—that blinds you? Will you sincerely ask Allah to remove that barrier today?
?
The Mu'tazila assumed that the same causes that prevent vision in the physical world also apply to Allah. What assumptions about Allah might you be projecting from your own limited experience? Are you willing to let go of those assumptions and submit to what Allah has revealed, even if it challenges your reasoning?
?
If your vision of Allah in the Hereafter depends on the absence of an opposite in your soul, what opposite are you nurturing right now—doubt, sin, or attachment to this world? What step will you take this week to uproot it?

Inconsistent Methods and the Real Cause of Non-Vision
Nasafi accuses the Mu'tazila of inconsistency and refutes the claim that fineness prevents vision due to weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi exposes the inconsistency in Mu'tazili reasoning and refutes the claim that fineness prevents vision due to weakness of eyesight.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila are inconsistent: they apply one rule in the witnessed but not in the unseen.

Nasafi continues his refutation by pointing out that the Mu'tazila themselves do not consistently apply their own method of analogy. He then addresses a specific argument from a follower of al-Jubba'i who claims that fineness prevents vision due to weakness of eyesight. Through a dialectical exchange, Nasafi shows that the real cause is the opposite of vision, not fineness.

Guided Path
1
Observe the accusation of inconsistency
Read the part where Nasafi accuses the Mu'tazila of inconsistency in their judgment about substance/accident and knowledge. They apply one rule in the witnessed but not in the unseen.
2
Examine the argument from al-Jubba'i's follower
The follower claims that fineness of a body prevents vision because it causes weakness in eyesight. Note this claim.
3
Follow the dialectical exchange
Nasafi asks a series of conditional questions about whether weakness can be removed while fineness remains, and what follows. Trace the logic to see how fineness is not the cause.
This shows that if weakness is removed, vision occurs despite fineness.

ويقال : كيف تنتفي رؤية الدقيق والبعيد من العين لا بضد جعل محل الرؤية الحالة في عين الرائي والغيبة الحالة في غيره؟ وكيف يجوز أن ينتفي شيء بوجود عرض في غير محله؟ ثم يقال لهم: ولم زعمتم أن ما يجوز أن يرى إذ لم نره فإنما لم نره للخلال التي عددتموها من الدقة والحجاب والغيبة والبعد واللطافة؟ فإن ادعوا أن فيما بينا كذلك. قيل لهم: ولم زعمتم أن ذلك فيما بينا كذلك مع مخالفتنا إياكم فيما ادعيتموه ومنعنا أن يكون شيء من ذلك لا يرى كما ذكرتم في الشاهد وإحالتنا انعدام الرؤية إلى وجود ضد الرؤية. ثم يقال لهم: لم يزل رأيكم الرجوع إلى مجرد الوجود والعمي عن حقائق العلل ولم قضيتم بذلك إذا وجدتم كذلك ثم يطالبون بأن يقضوا أن الشيء في الغائب لا يكون إلا جوهرا أو عرضا والقائم بنفسه لا يكون إلا جوهراً لوجودهم كذلك، ثم من غباوتهم أنهم وجدوا في الشاهد كل عالم موصوفا بالعلم ثم لم يقضوا بذلك في الغائب، وإن تعلق العلم بالعالم تعلق العلل بالمعلولات ثم قضوا في الغائب بما اقترن بآخر في الشاهد اقتران الوجود، وهذا مما لا يخفى فساده. ولما رأى بعض أصحاب الجبائي هذا الإلزام زعم أن دقة الجسم مانعة من الرؤية من أجل ضعف في البصر. فيقال لهم: أفيجوز ارتفاع الضعف مع وجود الدقة ؟ فإن قالوا: لا، أحالوا في كلامهم، لأن قيام معنى في محل لا يمنع من وجود عرض آخر في محل آخر. وإن قالوا نعم. قيل: إذا ارتفع الضعف هل نرى؟ فإن قالوا: لا، قيل لم وقد زال المانع؟ وإن قالوا: نعم، فقد رجعوا إلى الحق وأثبتوا الرؤية مع وجود الدقة فالانعدام حيث تنعدم لا يكون مضافا إلى الدقة بل على وجود ضد الرؤية، والله الموفق.
And it is said: How is vision of the fine and distant negated from the eye not by an opposite that makes the locus of vision the state in the eye of the seer and absence the state in another? And how can it be permissible that something be negated by the existence of an accident in a place other than its own? Then it is said to them: And why do you claim that what may be seen, when we have not seen it, we have not seen it only due to the defects you enumerated of fineness, veil, absence, distance, and subtlety? If they claim that in what we have explained it is so. It is said to them: And why do you claim that in what we have explained it is so, despite our opposition to you in what you have claimed and our denial that any of that is not seen as you mentioned in the witnessed world and our turning the non-existence of vision to the existence of the opposite of vision? Then it is said to them: Your opinion has not ceased to return to mere existence and blindness to the realities of causes, and why did you judge that when you found it so? Then they are demanded to judge that the thing in the unseen is nothing but substance or accident, and that which subsists by itself is nothing but substance, because they found it so. Then from their stupidity is that they found in the witnessed world every knower described by knowledge, then they did not judge that in the unseen, even though the attachment of knowledge to the knower is the attachment of causes to effects. Then they judged in the unseen by what was conjoined with another in the witnessed world with the conjunction of existence, and this is not hidden in its corruption. And when some of the followers of al-Jubba'i saw this obligating argument, he claimed that the fineness of the body prevents vision because of weakness in the eyesight. So it is said to them: Is it possible for weakness to be removed while fineness remains? If they say: No, they have made an impossibility in their speech, because the subsistence of a meaning in a locus does not prevent the existence of another accident in another locus. And if they say: Yes. It is said: If weakness is removed, do we see? If they say: No, it is said: Why, when the obstacle has been removed? And if they say: Yes, then they have returned to the truth and affirmed vision with the existence of fineness. So non-existence where it is non-existent is not attributed to fineness but to the existence of the opposite of vision, and God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 647

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazili argument that physical attributes such as fineness (smallness), veil, absence, distance, and subtlety prevent the vision of God. The author begins by posing a rhetorical question: how can the negation of vision be due to these attributes when vision is negated by an opposite state (like blindness) residing in the seer, not by an attribute in the object? He then challenges the Mu'tazila for assuming that the same causes that prevent vision in the witnessed world (e.g., smallness) apply to the unseen world (God). He points out their inconsistency: in the witnessed world, they judge that everything that exists is either substance or accident, but they do not apply this uniformly to the unseen. Specifically, they accept that a knower must have knowledge in the witnessed world, yet they deny that God (the Knower) must have knowledge in the unseen. The author then addresses a particular claim by a follower of al-Jubba'i that fineness prevents vision due to weakness in the eyesight. He asks whether weakness can be removed while fineness remains. If they say no, they contradict themselves because the existence of one attribute does not prevent another. If they say yes, then after weakness is removed, would we see? If they say no, the obstacle is removed so why not? If they say yes, they admit that fineness does not prevent vision and that the real cause is the opposite of vision (e.g., blindness). Thus the author concludes that the non-existence of vision is due to the presence of its opposite, not to the attributes they listed.

عرض
علم
ضعف
بصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fineness of an object directly prevents vision by causing weakness in eyesight.
Correction: Fineness does not prevent vision; if weakness is removed, vision occurs despite fineness. The real cause is the opposite of vision.

Checkpoint
According to the dialectical exchange, what happens if weakness is removed while fineness remains?
A) Vision still does not occur because fineness is the obstacle
B) Vision occurs, proving fineness is not the cause
C) It is impossible for weakness to be removed
D) Fineness also disappears

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila are inconsistent in their reasoning, and fineness does not prevent vision; the real cause is the opposite of vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue about matters of faith, do you ever hold Allah to a different standard than you do people? The Mu'tazila accepted that every knower has knowledge, but denied it for Allah. In what area of your life might you be inconsistent in applying the same principle to Allah as to creation? Will you repent and correct this today?
?
Nasafi shows that the Mu'tazila's claim about fineness preventing vision was based on a false assumption about weakness. Are there cherished beliefs you hold that you are unwilling to examine with logic and evidence, even if they might be false? Can you humble yourself to seek truth even at the cost of your pride?
?
The true cause of non-vision is not the external obstacle (fineness) but the internal opposite (ضد الرؤية). In your spiritual life, do you blame external circumstances for your lack of closeness to Allah, when the real obstacle is inside you—your sins, distractions, or indifference? What step will you take today to remove that internal barrier?

The Compensation for Losing the Vision of God
Nasafi answers the objection that deprivation of the vision of God entails a loss of pleasure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the claim that deprivation of the vision of God diminishes Divine blessings, using the concepts of compensation and turning the objection back on opponents.

Core Idea
Being turned away from seeing God is not a loss but an increase through compensation.

This passage addresses a common Mu'tazili objection: if the vision of God is the greatest pleasure in Paradise, then being deprived of it would be a diminishment of blessings. Nasafi not only refutes this but turns the argument back on the objectors. He also critiques a faulty theological premise about the nature of pleasure.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
The passage ends abruptly—think of it as a continuous argument that likely continues.
2
Identify the three main moves in Nasafi's response
Look for where he clarifies the discussion, gives the compensation argument, and then turns the objection back. Also note the critique of al-Qalanisi.
These moves form the core of his refutation.
3
Reflect on the analogy of the thousand dinars and the dirham
Consider why adding a dirham to a thousand dinars is better than just a thousand. How does this illustrate compensation?
The analogy shows that receiving additional pleasures on top of the vision (or instead of it) is an increase.
4
Notice how Nasafi turns the objection back on his opponents
He asks: if the pleasure of seeing the Prophet is also among the best, then being turned away from it must be a decrease. Whatever the opponents answer applies to the vision of God.
This clever move shifts the burden of proof.
5
Consider the critique of al-Qalanisi's theory of generated pleasure
Al-Qalanisi claimed pleasure is generated from vision. If true, no vision means no pleasure. But the Sunni position says pleasure is not generated, so it can exist without vision.
This shows that the entire objection rests on a specific theory of pleasure that the Sunnis reject.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the answer to their saying: That vision according to you is a matter of reward and its pleasure is above all other pleasures; with its removal, blessings diminish. Based on their assertion, if we assume that the discussion is about proving that the Essence is visible, not about the existence of vision necessarily in the abode of recompense, the objection is repelled. Then we say: The matter is not as you claim; when one is turned away from seeing God (meaning that God creates the opposite of His vision in their eyes), pleasure does not cease but exists, and he is turned by that pleasure to the pleasure of eating, drinking, and marriage. So they return to an increase, not a decrease. This is like a man to whom a donor gives a thousand dinars, then after that gives him a dirham; the dirham together with a thousand dinars is better than a thousand alone. Then this question is turned back upon them: It is said to them: If the pleasure of seeing the Prophet (peace be upon him) is among the best pleasures of Paradise, then when they are turned away from seeing him, they must return to a decrease. So whatever they answer to that, it is our answer to them. Then this speech perhaps applies to Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi, where he claimed that the pleasure of vision is generated from vision and resides in the soul of the seer due to a strong nature in the living seer. If the matter is as he holds, then it is inconceivable that pleasure occurs after the removal of the vision that generates pleasure, and there is no existence of the generated without the generating cause. As for us, since we do not say that it is generated, it is conceivable that it exists after the removal of vision, so
Source pages p. 648

Commentary
This passage answers a Mu'tazili objection: if the vision of God is the greatest pleasure in Paradise, then being deprived of it would be a decrease in blessings. The author first clarifies that the discussion is about proving that God is visible in essence, not about the actual occurrence of vision in the afterlife. Then he argues that when God turns the blessed away from seeing Him (by creating the opposite of vision in their eyes), they do not lose pleasure; rather, they are given other pleasures (eating, drinking, marriage) as compensation. This is an increase, not a decrease, just as a man who receives a thousand dinars and then a dirham has more than just the thousand. The author then turns the objection back on the opponents: if the pleasure of seeing the Prophet is also among the greatest pleasures, then being turned away from it would also be a decrease; whatever they answer to that applies to the vision of God. Finally, the author critiques Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi's view that the pleasure of vision is generated (mutawallid) from the vision itself. If that were true, then without vision there could be no pleasure. But the Sunni position (the author's) does not hold that pleasure is generated, so it is possible to have pleasure even after vision ceases (i.e., through other means).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Being deprived of the vision of God in Paradise necessarily means losing all pleasure and experiencing a decrease in reward.
Correction: Nasafi argues that God compensates with other pleasures, so the blessed experience an increase, not a decrease. The Sunni position does not tie pleasure exclusively to vision.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the claim that losing the vision of God diminishes blessings?
A) The vision of God is not a pleasure at all
B) The blessed are given other pleasures as compensation, so it is an increase
C) They will never be turned away from seeing God
D) The pleasure of vision is not real

Key Takeaway
Nasafi demonstrates that being turned away from the vision of God does not diminish reward; rather, it is compensated with other pleasures, and the Sunni position does not tie pleasure exclusively to vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to be deprived of the vision of God in the Hereafter, would you truly believe that God's compensation is sufficient? Reflect on a time you felt you lost something precious in this life. Did you trust that God would compensate you with something better? How does this lesson challenge your view of divine justice and mercy?
?
Nasafi argues that when turned away from seeing God, one is given other pleasures. But is there any pleasure that can truly replace the vision of God? What does that say about your own longing for God? Are you content with lesser pleasures now, or do you yearn for the ultimate encounter?
?
The opponent's objection assumes that loss of vision is a decrease. But Nasafi says it's an increase. How does this reshape your understanding of God's generosity? Are you willing to accept that even what seems like deprivation is actually a gift? What would it take for you to truly believe that in your heart?
